“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at SY DNEY,N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Is only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Yoke.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education,
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical, It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it, was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily piper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper; and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushmau of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of alVjournals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Church-goer ; “ Last Sunday, my pastor
dwelt on the familiar commonplace about the
elevation of woman. Now, I fail to see it. Take
up Lander’s ‘Pericles and Aspasia,’and ask how
far woman has advanced since that age. She now
has to battle with man, and because a few George
Eliots or George Sands come out on top, we hear
suph a lot about what she is doing. Ask the poor
toilers and moilers, nob the successful few.”
Thus the Singapore Free Press :
N.S.W. is officially set down as having a total wealth
of £412,484,000—£368 per head. Considering that the
colony is non-producing and is essentially a “middle-
man, that the great majority of the civil Europeans, say
SO per cent, are men on salary and contract, and that the
vast bulk of the population is of the coolie class or only a
grade above (hat, and largely of a migratory or non-
domiciled character, we should not be surprised to find
the private property well under £lO a head.
Next 1
Pondoland, the latest slice of Africa grabbed
by Britain, is a fine tract of grazing-country, on
which J. Bull has for years had his lascivious eye.
It lies on the S.E. coast between “British”
Caffraria and Griqualand West. The Pondos,
bastard Hottentots, are the most miserably super-
stitious of all African tribes. They are run by
witch-doctors or “seers ’’ and, in a minor degree,
by a potentate named Sigcaus. Should the King’s
dog go lame, or some of his cattle be lost, a
, lseer ’’ is sent for and commissioned to “smell
it out.” The old harridan sniffs about and reports
that some one—generally a personal enemy—has
the animal; and the accused is forth-
with mutilated. The reports of these horrors
have shocked the sensitive Bull and, although the
cable doesn t say so, this is probably the excuse
given for grabbing Pondoland, for there doesn’t
seem to be any other.
All N.S.VV. electoral candidates now writ-

the speech of Cobar candidate Willis :
My reason for offering myself is not because 1 am sel-
fish ; it is not because I am ambitious and wish to rise at
the expense of the workers, nor is it because I had my
eye on £3OO a year and a free railway pass. If any of
these were my motives then I would he unworthy of the
name of an Australian and a man ! (Hear, hear, and
? P ,fu Se ') My motives are far different, I recognise the
fact that unless fresh men are elected—men who have the
welfare and interests of the labouring masses indelibly
stamped on their hearts—the country will go headlong to
destruction. (Applause.) That is my reason for offering
myself, and now is the time when you should rise up in
your strength—and you are strong, stronger than you
know of and hurl these howling, blatant idiots into
everlasting obscurity (applause) and elect in their stead
men who would legislate in the interests of the people.

A Maoriland paper describes the fall of a
once-eminent citizen who lately did the customary
Pacific slope;—
Hb commenced life as a little grocer in one of the
very humblest streets, and he attended loyally to his
business, sanding the sugar and saying his prayers
and coming home to tea with utmost regularity. Then,
' n ., an , , evil moment, Mrs. Johnson persuaded J. to buy a
tali chimney-pot hat and to take a class at Sunday-school.
, “ proved his ruin. It led him into temptation. It
led him on to try for municipal honors. It, like a will-o’-
the wisp, decoyed him into longing for the Mayoralty.
J* rom that moment he was lost. He threw his honest
apron away. He refused longer to weigh out half-pounds
of sugar and ounces of tea. He engaged an assistant.
He put on his coat. Then he took to wearing that hat
everyday and to swelling about the streets. He bought a
bigger but not a better grocer’s business. He built a
villa, and Mrs. J. had her “Hat ’Omes.’’ Then he cut
soap and candles, sold the businesses, and blossomed
forth with forty pony power as a “ land and commission
agent. Then ! 1
“Mrs. J. persuaded him.” The same good, old
wheeze. When a man does anything which turns
out successful he always did it himself, and when
he does some utter foolishness it always transpires
somehow that his wife persuaded him. “ She
offered me the apple and I did eat.”
Testimonial received by the Australian
agents for a well-known and much-advertised
patent remedy :
Dear Sir,— l have suffered for many years from lumbago
and other rheumatic affections, and at last began to con-
sider my case incurable. I tried many doctors and paid
large sums in fees, but with no appreciable benefit.
Sometimes I was a little better and sometimes a little
worse, but on the whole the doctors did me no service. I
also tried a good many patent remedies, many of which
were said to be infallible for my complaint, with the same
reSult '* ast “ friend urged me strongly to try your
world-famed cure. I was reluctant at first, being about
tired of medicines, but ultimately I consented. 1 tried
six bottles altogether. It didn’t do me any good either.
You ought to be sent to gaol for getting money under
false pretences. Your medicine is a fraud, and the
testimonials you publish must be bogus for no one could
be cured by such rubbish. I will call on you
some day when I am in town, for I want a
good look at the hog who has the effrontery
to advertise such a thing. Why don’t you give it up and
be a burglar, or something honest, instead of swindling
the halt and the blind and the bed-ridden f I wouldn’t
like to be seen dead in the same street with you.
Yours, &0., . *
The above is probably a fair sample of the joys of
being a patent-medicine fiend. It would be
interesting to know bow many such documents
come to hand and are judiciously suppressed. The
unpublished correspondence of a patent-medicine
man should be lovely reading.

tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

The question, “ What constitutes a ]our
nalist ? ” has been settled to the satisfaction of a
Queensland R.O. cleric, who, in a recent sermon,
described the scribe as “a man you pick out of
the gutter and can buy both body and soul for a
glass of rum.” It was possibly this variety of
“ journalist ” who wrote in the Australian (R. 0.)
the attack upon Gresley Lukin, which cost the
paper about JlllOO.
At one small S.A. township, where there
ja no courthouse, the Government hires the
Anglican church to fine drunks in, at the moderate
rental of ss. per week. The justices sit round the
communion-table, and the drunk is located in
the front pew. Then the arresting constable gets
up in the reading desk and fills the sacred edifice
with an account of the horrible language the
prisoner used when ho was arrested, and the
echoes of it get lost among the recesses of the
roof, whence they emerge to curse unexpectedly in
the midst of next Sunday’s service.
Blowitz, of the Times, relates novelist
Barrie, was not present at a certain conversation
between Bismarck and Rosebery, but he wrote
about it. Later on Rosebery met Blowitz and
complimented him on the report. “ Ah, it was
all right, was it ? ” asked Bowitz beaming. “No,”
replied Rosebery, “it was all wrong.” “Then, if
Bismarck did not say that to you,” remarked
Blowitz regally, “ I know he intended to say it.”
Colonel Olcott, the wavy-haired Theoso-
phist—he looked like a cross between Charles
Bright, Canon Kemmis and the late Major Nel-
gon—who lately visited Australia, talks of him-
self and Mrs. Annie Besant“ We may, I hope
and pray, henceforth resemble in this movement
the Aryan god, who is dual when looked at from
two aspects but when properly understood is but
one and indivisible.” Madame Blavatsky was in
the habit of treating the gassy Colonel with
scant courtesy. She was known to interrupt one
of his long-winded and unintelligible discourses
with the very affectionate and gratifying remark,
“My dear, you are a fool!” Which he was. But
as he was also a pompous old fellow and good-
looking, they “swallowed ” him and hung on his
words. For all his words conveyed, they might
as well have hung on to a goat’s beard.
Dear Bulletin, —Be Judge Windeyer’s
proposed adoption in Australia of the French
criminal-identification system-this may interest
your readers. Measurements are taken of, 1,
height; 2, arm-spread ; 3, head-length • 4, head-
width; 5, mid-finger of left hand ; 6, left foot;
then eye-colour is referred to lof 7 shades. Thus,
Ist measurement of a suspect directs investigator
to a particular batch of photographs ; 2nd do., to
a certain section of that; 3rd, to a subdivision
again of that, and so on, till, when the “ final ” is
reached, the prisoner has only to be compared
with half-a-dozen portraits or so, instead of with
as many thousands. There is a case on record in
which, out of nearly 7000 photographs, the only
one left after the elimination was that of the
prisonerhimself! “The French are so unpractical,
don’t you know !”— Millimetre.
All the rage in Paris. Send 13 penny
stamps for latest Parisian preventative. National
Drug Co., Russell-st., Melbourne.
Nearly every paper in Maoriland recently
received a circular from an utterly unknown
Englishman named Smith, or Jones, or something
to that effect, which circular the editor was re-
quested to publish gratis in his news columns as a
matter of public interest. This document sets
forth at great length that the aforesaid nonentity
is about to visit Maoriland, that his great object
in doing so is to see his beloved mother and sister
who live somewhere in the province, and to visit
his father’s grave, which is also located there. All
his relatives are in big capitals, and so is the
grave. He adds that he is “ aged 44, active, and
a temperance worker,” and that “he may easily
l>e persuaded to take up his permanent residence
in the colony, and after a time seek the favour of
I e oming a member of the House of Representa-
tives, or any office where he can be of ser-
vice.” An accompanying photograph represents
a straggly, goggle-eyed person, with wild-looking
whiskers flying loosely in the blast. The photo,
is also intended to be reproduced gratis as a
matter of public interest.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
't his offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tim Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will he regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 14, 1894.
Notice to Litigauts.
A few weeks ago The Bulletin appeared in
the courts as defendant in a libel-suit.
There were no points of special importance
in this particular suit, in fact it was about
as plain and as profoundly solemn as any-
thing of the kind could well be ; and the
case is alluded to here, not by reason of its
intrinsic interest, but merely as a gi-otesque
example of the costliness of the law. And,
even in this respect, it was an exceedingly
ordinary event and much less grotesque an
example than the average, and the only
reason why it is selected as a text is that the
bill of costs is readily available, whereas in
many other cases, which might have much
more effectually pointed a moral, that
awesome document is not in The Bulle-
tin’s, possession. The trouble in question
arose through sundry complimentary re-
marks which this paper passed upon a Mr.
(John) Marsland, of Melbourne. The re-
marks were strictly complimentary—or,
at all events, they were so intended—but,
through a confusion of Christian names, the
subject of them was mixed up with another
Mr. Marsland, then in legal difficulties
through sheltering a fugitive banker. The
Bulletin had no possible animosity against
—in fact, nobody directly connected with it
had ever even seen—either of these gentle-
men ; and, on learning that a mistake had
been committed, it was perfectly willing to
apologise (as a matter of fact it did at once
fully and freely apologise), and pay reason-
able compensation for any injury that had
been done.
» • #
The plaintiff, however, had the misfortune
to be cursed with the possession of certain
friends, who strongly advised him to take
legal proceedings and claim a much larger
amount in damages than this paper was
willing to pay. If the result proves any-
thing in particular, it proves that no litigant
should have friends. The ideal litigant
is an orphan and absolutely without relatives
or acquaintances or well-wishers of any kind
to advise him to his own undoing. Mr.
Marsland, therefore, took legal proceedings
and was awarded £lOO damages and costs.
The damages were about twice as much as
The Bulletin was qirepared to offer, in the
first instance, and without litigation; and the
bill of plaintiff s costs as presented to defend-
ant by plaintiff s lawyer amounted to exactly
£459 2s. Bd. Therefore, it would appear
at first sight as if plaintiff had profited con-
siderably by his recourse to the law. The
average litigant always regards a verdict of
£lOO and costs as £lOO actual cash, and only
learns some time afterwards that it almost
always means a dead loss, which loss may
amount to anything from a few pounds up
to thousands.
* # «
In the first place, plaintiff s bill of costs
was “ taxed ” in the usual fashion by the
prothonotary. The business of this officer
is to strike out of the account all items
which he regards as inadmissible. In the
present instance he struck out £273 11s. Bd.,
which means that out of Mr. Marsland’s
legal expenses The Bulletin, which had
lost the case, had to pay £lB5 11s., while
Mr. Marsland, who had won it, was left to
pay the balance, £273 lls. Bd. Therefore
his verdict of £lOO was immediately swal-
lowed up, and Mr. Marsland was still
£173 lls. Bd. out of pocket in actual
cash on the whole deal. There was
nothing exceptional about this—in fact, the
situation, so far as plaintiff was concerned,
was rather more favourable than is usually
the case. No plaintiff who understands the
utter ■ incomprehensibleness of the law ever
expects to get anything*out of a libel verdict
—in fact, he has no reason to expect any-
thing good out of any kind of a verdict. The
awarding of costs is, at the best, little more
than a formality. The process of “ taxation ”
almost always leaves the winner to pay a
large portion of his own bill, and it must be
a great and gorgeous verdict in a most econo-
mically-conducted case where the damages
awarded cover even the winner’s share of
the expenses.
» « »
But even this is not all. In addition to
the fact that the successful litigant seldom
gets more than half the costs which the
Court nominally awards, there are others
which the Court does not even profess to
award him. These come under the head of
special costs between solicitor and client, and
their amount is eminently uncertain. In a
small and very ordinary case, however, they
easily amount to £lOO or more. Therefore,
the ordinary libel verdict is practically to
the effect that plaintiff has been wronged by
defendant, and that the Court therefore grants
him £lOO damages, and leaves him to pay
his own costs to the extent of, perhaps,
£350. The legal victory is thus a minus
quantity of £250; the successful litigant
never receives one penny of the alleged
verdict; his time and trouble and anxiety
all go for naught, and he is possibly harried
and sold up and made bankrupt and prac-
tically fired into the street by his solicitor in
his efforts to recover the balance of account.
This is the blissful prospect which the law
holds out to the man who wins a libel suit,
and even if winning were a matter of abso-
lute certainty there is nothing in it to induce
any person who is clothed and in his right
mind to unnecessarily resort to litigation.
But even this joyous result is an uncertain
thing and hard to arrive at, and it is only by
much effort that a plaintiff achieves that happy
condition in which the damages awarded
him are all annexed by his attorney, and he
has mayhap to borrow from the usurers to
pay up the rest of the costs. The law is
wonderfully and hideously uncertain, and
courts are full of surprising decisions, and,
as-a consequence, there is absolutely no case
on earth which is so good thac success can be
counted upon with anything like certainty,
or any case so bad that it may not crush a
dividend—for the lawyers. Therefore,
while the best the average plaintiff can hope
for is that the damages awarded him will pay
part of his expenses, there is always a pos-
sibility that he will be awarded no damages
at all, and will be ordered to pay the costs
on both sides, and will be ruined by two
solicitors instead of one. Or, again, even
in the event of success, defendant may
prove to be insolvent, and the verdict may
be irrecoverable ; or he may appeal from one
court to another, and either get the verdict
reversed, or pile up expenses till his
opponent is compelled to drop the case from
sheer financial exhaustion ; or he may drag
it out with all the hundred different delays
and postponefnents which the wealthy suitor
can always command, till his opponent fades
away from worry and that old age which
comes of long-continued disappointment.
* « *
Therefore the plaintiff, under ordinary
conditions, has four separate prospects
before him : (1) He may lose, no matter
how good his case may be, and be ruined
with bills of costs ; (2) he may win in the
first instance, and be afterwards cheated
out of his verdict through appeals and
legal technicalities, and be ruined also;
(3) he may win and fail to recover any-
thing, owing to his opponent’s insolvency—
in which case he stands in much the same
position as if he lost—and be ruined ; and
(4) he may win, and get a verdict and costs
—that is to say, what the Court terms costs,
but which are only a fraction of the real out-
lay ; and get his verdict paid promptly, with-
out demur, to the Uttermost farthing, and
still be ruined in the same old way. The
man in humble circumstances who takes pro-
ceedings in the higher Courts, where the
expensive horsehair barrister must be laid on
before the legal mill will begin to revolve, is
tolerably certain to be ruined in any and
every case. The only man who has any sane
or reasonable hope of making anything
out of law-proceedings is the one who
can conduct his own case—the gritty
individual who knows enough law to
be his own barrister and his own soli-
citor as well —who is a practised speaker,
s > that he can put his own case as effectively
to the jury as any costly Q.C. could do, and
who has the requisite nerve and presence of
mind to hold his own against an array of
arrogant legal authorities who are calculated
to strike terror into the inexperienced. Also,
in addition to all these qualifications, he
should be a person with no wife or family, or
other ties of any kind to hamper his move-
ments, and with no assets which he cannot*
pack at the shortest notice into his carpet-
bag, so that, if things go against him, he can
fold his tent and steal away, leaving his
opponent’s unpaid bill behind him. To such
a person a law-suit may be a legitimate
gamble ; to anybody else it is profound and
unspeakable foolishness.
The Bulletin has not, and never had,
any feelings towards its late antagonist, Mr.
Marsland, save those of perfect good-will.
It had no personal quarrel with that gentle-
man, and was perfectly willing to recognise
that it had unwittingly done him an injury
and to compensate him for it, had he been
disposed to negotiate without recourse to the
law-courts. Also, it is not now squealing
over its monetary loss—it only resurrects the
story of the case as the most readily avail-
able example of the utter iniquity of the
existing legal system. The cumbrous
formalism of the civil law and the extortions of
the close legal corporation have made justice
a commodity which overwhelms the just
and the unjust both alike. In a very large
proportion of the cases which come before the
superior courts thelegalbrotherhood swallows
everything, and the nominal winner and the
nominal loser lose pretty much alike. The
civil law, in fact, exists for the benefit of the
legal brotherhood, and when it is satisfied
there is practically nothing left. If the
amounts of all the verdicts given in any one
year in all the superior civil courts of Aus-
tralia, and all the legal costs connected there-
with, were added up, it is almost safe to
assert that the costs would be very much
the larger item of the two every time. If
now and then there should be an exception
to this rule, then something has gone wrong
with the legal machine, and it wants to be
overhauled and repaired.
The Opium Bogey.
That keen advertiser, Mr. Quong Tart, the
Scotch-Chiuese gentleman who, in displaying to
the world the ethical and commercial value of
Presbyterianism has earned the undying love of
his countrymen, has started another crusade
against the opium traffic. Quong Tart’s
spasms of public virtue appear to culminate in
ten-year cycles, the last of any importance
being in 1881, when a police sub-inspector went
round the New South Wales Chinese camps
with the strict Q.T., and made eleven recom-
mendations, among which prohibition of the
opium traffic had no place whatever.
* • *
The terrible iniquities of the opium traffic
exist only in the imagination of the wary
Quong and his friends, the old ladies of
both sexes. The 1884 inspector shows this
when he says in one breath that all Chinese
smoke opium, and, in the next, that the New
South Wales Chinese, with few exceptions,
deserve the character of belonging to the most
industrious race in the world. The evidence
taken before the Royal Commission on opium
in India has shown it over and over again.
There is overwhelming proof that opium-eaters
are not more base, or weak, or vile than other
people ; that opium does not rob them of their
health, strength and spirit; and that without
it they couldn’t endure the tyranny of a rule
which has crushed them with taxation, crippled
them with debt, and exacts a tribute of
£18,000,000 a year in gold. Gov. Des Yieux,
formerly of Hong Kong, with as wide an experi-
ence of Eastern nations as any man living,
affirms that opium, no doubt, like alcohol, does
harm in cases of inordinate excess, but to the
very great majority it is, to say the least,
innocent. The shame of the traffic from India,
which Miss Ackermann and the shrieking sister-
hood work for all it is worth, is a pure fiction
to make the cash flow into missionary coffers.
The opium imported from India is far too dear
for most Chinese consumers, and most of the
opium used in China is grown on Chinese soil.
Victoria, the capital of Hongkong, has a popu-
lation of 150,000 crammed into a space two
miles long and J-mile broad, under the most
insanitary conditions, in a much hotter climate
than Sydney, yet the death-rate of recent years
has been 22 per 1000—only 3 per 1000 more
than Sydney. Sir Thomas Wade, formerly
British ambassador at Pekin, says England has
not supplied China with 5 per cent, of the
opium consumed during 120 years of trade. To
stop the trade would be the same as declaring
in England that all forms of tobacco might be
imported, except Havanna cigars, and all forms
of alcohol, except French brandy.
* # #
Opium-smoking is conceded to be worse than
opium-eating, but that is not the point. The
point is that “human life is subject to such
manifold wretchedness that all nations have
invented a something, liquid or solid, to pro-
duce a brief oblivion. Poppies, barley, grasses,
sugar, pepper and a thousand other things,
have been squeezed, pressed, pounded and
purified, to produce this temporary happiness.”
If you could succeed in taking away opium
from the Chinese they would find a worse
substitute. But in Australia, at any rate,
this is impossible. Opium is so small in
bulk and so easily smuggled, and there is such
a length of unguarded territory, that it would
be utterly impossible to prevent its introduc-
tion. The only effect of forbidding the traffic
in New South Wales would be to raise the price,
and make the Chinese poorer for the benefit of
their Queensland and Victorian kindred.
/ * * #
When the astute Quong Tart says that on
opium rests all vice, immorality and corruption
with the Chinese, he talks deplorable nonsense.
If with every thousand male Chinese landed in
Australia comes but one Chinese woman, the
demoralisation of European women fol-
lows as a matter of course. It is evident
that the opium agitation has no basis.
But if Quong Tart and his abettors will go
a step further back—will prevent, instead of
trying to cure, and suppress, in place of the
opium, the Chinese who use the opium, The
Bulletin will back them up for all it is worth,
knowing that, in spite of individual virtue, the
Chinese in the mass are what America has long
ago found them, a cancer in a civilised com-
munity ; and a degradation and a danger to
white manhood and womanhood in Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Voice from Cootamnudra.
Mr. 0. H. S. Chauncy writes indignantly from
the far-off wilds of Cootamundra (N.S.W.) :
Dear Bulletin, — A paragraph appearing in your issue
of 17th march as to solicitors who misappropriate trust
moneys is sheer rot, and you must well know it, unless
you are blind, deaf, and dumb. You wind up by saying,
“the attorney who embezzles should go to gaol”—and
don’t they go to gaol? What about Broad's case, quite
recently ?—but I suppose for the sake of sensation it suits
you to forget that. You are not alone amongst the
Sydney press in writing balderdash about the privileges
of the legal profession. Some years ago the D.T. raised a
howl on the iniquity of the system whereby a certain soli
citor could only be struck off the rolls, instead of being
imprisoned. The real fact is that solicitors are liable
with everyone else to the same punishments under the
criminal law for the same offences, and the penalty of
being struck off the rolls is an additional one which
attaches to solicitors and not to others. You can “ jug ”
a butcher, or a journalist for the matter of that, but you
can’t take his occupation for ever from him. The
“privileged ” solicitor, as you call him, is, however, liable
to both punishments, and if the aggrieved party chooses
to select the latter and leave the former alone, that is
entirely his own look-out. Where are your privileges
now? The fact is that the word “privilege” is first
cousin to the word “monopoly,” and both these words
suit your tone so well that you can’t resist the temptation
to make use of them, even at the expense of the facts.
The Bulletin desires to say a very few words
in reply to this infuriated contributor. First
of all, in 99 cases out of 100 the dishonest
attorney doesn’t go to gaol. The ordinary
swindler gets there pretty regularly, but the
“jugging” of the legal swindler is almost as
rare as a snowball in Gehenna. Broad’s case,
which involved a very large sum, was one lone-
some incident in a century. The regular pro-
cedure is for the swindle to be proved up to the
hilt; then for the Judge, with tears in his
eyes, to suspend the offender for six months, or
two years, or more, and after that the Crown
Prosecutor, or Attorney-General, or whatever
it is, closes the other eye and does nothing.
The fact that fraudulent solicitors are “ liable ”
to go to gaol doesn’t matter so long as they
don’t go. For the rest, the ordinary clerk or
book-keeper who does a term in Darling-
hurst or Pentridge has his occupation taken
from him about as successfully, in the
great majority of cases, as if he was
“ struck off the roll ” also. Moreover,
the solicitor’s profession isn’t altogether
taken from him ; he can still be a law-clerk, or
he can practise—provided he can get clients—
under cover of the name of some needy attor-
ney who hasn’t been struck off. Furthermore,
in a very large proportion of cases, the legal
robber only suffers a temporary suspension ; he
is merely forbidden to practise for 12 months or
so in cases where the common lay thief would
be sent to prison for three years sure. The
Bulletin recommends these facts to the serious
consideration of the irate gentleman from Coo-
tamundra, and trusts that he will think over
them hard.
“ Evidence as to Character.”
A few days ago Judge Stephen (N.S.W.) rose
in insurrection against that ghastly farce, “evi-
dence as to character.” It was about time.
When a prisoner has been proved to be an
exceedingly bad character, the defence calls
certain people to testify, in effect, that they
weren’t aware of the fact till it was discovered.
Then the Judge or magistrate has a favourable
opening—if the prisoner is a friend of his, or a
friend of a friend of his, or a person of influence
or “ respectability ” —to reduce the sentence
to a minimum on account of “ previous good
characteror, if the prisoner is nobody in
particular, he can point out in a stern tone of
voice that he is being tried for his crime,
not for his previous character, and that
the latter has absolutely nothing at all to do
with the case. “Evidence as to character”
is the device by which the w'ell-connected
criminal dodges the law; it is practically
of no use at all to the person with
no connections, for its value depends en-
tirely on the status of the person who gives
the evidence. Thus the prisoner of “social
standing ” calls a Cabinet Minister, a parson or
two, and one or two wealthy bankers and
politicians to solemnly testify that they didn’t
know he was a bad character till it became
known, and the average judge is impressed by
the assurance of their ignorance ; but when
the other kind of person calls a hawker, a man
who is out of work, and two pick-and-shovel
labourers to swear to exactly the same effect
their ignorance produces no impression what-
ever. The court simply concludes that they
look like the sort of people from whom
ignorance might be expected. Evidence as to
character, in fact, is simply an attempt to
corrupt the administration of justice. Every
person can find a large number of important
people who didn’t previously know any-
thing against him; it is only a question
of bringing sufficient influence to bear
upon them to induce them to state the
utterly ridiculous and uninteresting circum-
stance upon oath. And this is where the social


difference comes in, for while the man of
“standing,” who has been bowled out, calls
evidence as to character, in the case of the man
of no standing a policeman simply gets up and
reluctantly admits that there are no previous
convictions against the prisoner. Which is just
tlie same tiling under another name. Even
when ten influential citizens of vast circumfer-
ence get up in succession and testify as to
character, it really only means that there are
no previous known convictions against the
prisoner.
A Religious Crisis.
The great and godly Chirnside family, of
Werribee (Vic.) district, has, in effect, ordered
a new Presbyterian shepherd for its private
soul. It is not generally known that the
Chirnside monarchy takes a deep interest in
religion, but such would seem to be the case.
Eor the past three years, then, these lamentable
capitalists have glared with disfavour upon the
local pastor, who depended upon them to the
extent of £l5O a year and a manse. He was too
broad, or not deep enough for the reigning De-
fender of the Faith and Court, or, perhaps, his
interpretation of certain psalms differed from
theirs —at any rate, they have been compelled
to attend the Church of England, “much
against their inclination,” rather than shed
any further glory upon the ministry of their
vassal. Finally, they intimated their intention
to withdraw the subsidy of £l5O a year, and
close the manse, unless the Rev. White be
hurled from his present pulpit within a given
period. Failing obedience to this latter in-
struction they will go over in a body, accom-
panied by a full band, to the Church of England
aforesaid. It transpires that the inferior mem-
bers of Mr. White’s flock, more especially the
denizens of Little Riverand Lara, approve of him
altogether, and, therefore, disapprove of the
Chibnsides in this spiritual matter. Geelong
Presbytery sent a deputation to enquire, and
the deputation reported that any shifting of
Rev. White would be, in worldly parlance,
“ rough ”on their respected brother. Several
alternative lines of policy were suggested—
firstly, “To take no action.” This would mean
that Mr. White’s salary will be reduced to
£l5O (the proud squattahs found half the in-
come, seemingly) without a manse, that the
Chirnside family will withdraw from the
Presbyterian Church, and that a number of
families who recently came to the Werribee
(and are under Chirnside influence ?) will be
lost to the Church. The inevitable results of
taking no action were too shocking to contem-
plate, so the Geelong Presbytery decided
to adopt another alternative —to with-
draw Mr. White from the Werribee por-
tion of his charge, and provide him with
a new church and residence in the neigh-
bouring district. By this ingenious device
the Chirnsides will be preserved to the Pres-
byterian faith of their ancestors, and left at
liberty to take another parson for their pro-
perty on approval. Henceforth no outside
interests can clash with the sovereign desires
of this delectable family, and any symptoms of
insubordination on the part of the new man of
God will ensure his instant dismissal. Once a
week he will call upon the lord of the manor
to enquire whether he has been preaching the
kind of “ Worrd ” that suits the family, and if
the family has had a bad Saturday afternoon
at the races, they may possibly get very cross
indeed with his Word. In fact, there is no
feeling certain, from this time forward, that
the Chirnsides will not suddenly form a sort
of fashionable theatre-party for joining the
Church of England. After which, the deluge !
“ A Shilling’s-worth of Sand.”
James Hynb, charged with removing soil to the extent of
o.ie shilling in value from the Daniel Cooper estate, was
fined 55., besides being ordered to pay the value and 4s.
lOd. costs, or 21 days. Sydney paper.
Daniel Cooper, aided by the law, received
hundreds of stolen acres of the public estate,
aud now gloats in luxurious absenteeism upon
the unearned increment. Yet nobody attempts
to bring that thief to justice. Daniel Cooper
does not even pretend to use for any useful
human purpose the earth thus legally stolen;
the Lean Man who carried off a shilling’s-worth
of sand proposed to increase the wealth of the
country by its use. Civilisation surpasses
savagery in stupidity. It would be a healthy
object-lesson for the democracy if 1000 unem-
ployed Sydney men would proceed in file
to the Daniel Cooper estate and steal
(Heaven bless the term !) a shilling’s-worth
of sand therefrom, seriatim, and “ take
out” the 21 days’ apiece. It would be
healthier still if some means could be found
by which the absentee Cooper could be made
to pay the country for the maintenance of such
thousand while in gaol. At almost every
Christmastide, one or more poor devils—
respectable heads of working families, as a rule
—are dragged before property-reverencing
salaried magistrates, under the pretence
of administering “justice,” for “stealing”
a few useless weeds from this same
Daniel Cooper estate, for the purposes of
Xmas decorations. There lie eternally the
ranges and gullies of heath-growing sand, im-
potent in ugliness, barren of all useful vegeta-
tion, shut out from all human use, and awaiting
only the trickery of the syndicate-mongers
who, watching the pimply spread of bricks and
mortar in the metropolitan area, will rig a land-
boom, and offer the greedy absentee thousands
of pounds sterling for every farthing which the
Daniel Cooper estate has cost him. Gigantic
robbery ! And how the mammoth robber pul-
verises the petty thief ! How his myrmidons
guard his shilling’s worth of sand !
A City of Desolation.
Melbourne Age has been exploring the more
utterly wretched suburbs of wretched Mel-
bourne, in order to test the accuracy of Premier
Patterson’s assertion, made over many expen-
sive public banquets, that the alleged distress
is exaggerated and unreal. The tale which that
papers unfolds is a harrowing affair, more like
a st £ r y of Jerusalem at the siege than a sketch
ot the biggest city of the youngest country upon
earth. Down in East Brunswick the Age struck
a new boom - township where there is no
drainage, no roads worth mentioning, and
where the houses, built five years ago of broken
brickbats and sand, are tottering wearily at all
sorts of angles, with their windows mostly gone
and their doors banging on one hinge, or propped
up with boulders. Many of these are let gratis
to an almost naked race of unemployed, who, in
return for the accommodation, scare oft' the
land-pirates who want to take the fences away
for firewood. No one has a full suit of clothes
in this locality, and very few have quite half a
suit. They sell tea, sugar, and most other
things by the half-pennyworth now in this land
of desolation, and when a householder buys a
whole pennyworth of anything at a time grave
doubts are entertained as to whether the coin
was honestly acquired. Then the Age started
to investigate South Melbourne and returned
with an even more ghastly report. “ Half of
South Melbourne is starving ” was the verdict.
This is another region where almost every
kind of food is sold by the halfpennyworth
because no one has a whole penny at one time.
In these two suburbs and in Richmond
the population consists mostly of women,
and most of them half-naked women with
no visible means of support. The men
are all away wandering over the country
looking for work and finding none. The
working-class suburbs look like Paraguay
after the war, when the male population was
practically extinct. There are women every-
where—prematurely old, tattered, gaunt, bony
and despairing. It is an unspeakably horrible
picture, and whoever has seen it longs to rise
and heave a brick at Patterson when that
iron-jawed politician gets up after the perpetual
banquet to state that it is necessary to take a
cheerful view of things, and to congratulate the
fat and shiny Gillies upon his “ services to the
country.”
Montgomery and Williams, the two Sydney
burglars, who grievously banged five policemen
in their efforts to escape, have been sentenced
to death. The capital charge against them was
“ wounding with intent to murder,” and no
sane individual believes that they did wound
with intent to murder. They simply wounded
with intent to get away, and the crime was one
which called for 10 years’ penal servitude. At
the same time, it was no worse than dozens of
other crimes which have ended in all manner of
sentences varying from six months down to 40s.
The case which, of all those within The Bulle-
tin’s recollection, most closely resembles it was
that of two rowdies who, two years ago, or
thereabouts, assaulted, as a mere matter of
amusement, a passing citizen in the neigh-
bourhood of Rushcutter’s Bay, Sydney, and
kicked him so brutally that he was laid up
for several weeks. The case was set down as
common assault, and the offenders were fined
either £2 or £s— The Bulletin doesn’t remem-
ber which for certain, but it inclines to the
idea that it was £2. The difference between
that 40s. episode and the matter of Mont-
gomery and Williams arose partly out of the
fact that the more recent case was accompanied
by a burglarious inroad on Property, and partly
out of Premier Dibbs’ uncalled-for interference
with the course of justice. But, so far as con-
cerned the assault—which was the only ques-
tion in the indictment—there was no difference
between the guilt of the men who are to be
hanged and that of the men who were fined
40s. New South Wales justice is a spasmodic,
hysterical affair, and is always wobbling
miserably between the gallows and 40s.
Will Sydney ratepayers note that Aldermen
Hughes, John Harris, Wayne, Landers and
Ives are the minority who don’t see why the
sisters and cousins and aunts of aldermen, and
their friends, and their friends’ friends, ad
infinitum, should get free tickets to the Town
Hall organ-recitals ? And will ' they note also,
for use at next elections, that all the other
aldermen are in favour of maintaining a system
which makes the average citizen pay for his
entertainment, and sends in for nothing the
aldermanic prot6g6, who can very well afford
to pay, too. Eight tickets for every concert
are sent to these aldermen, and some of them
get a good many more. At least 400 shilling
tickets are given free every week to the aider-
men’s pets, equal to £lO4O a year—or twice the
salary of the organist ! “We must have our
little pickings !”
The bank-clerk is still utilised in Australian
courts as a signature-expert in preference to
the lithographic artist who, as a professional
copier of signatures, is surely more likely to
know what he is talking about. The other day
in a Sydney will-case three bank experts swore
that a certain signature was forged, and another
swore it wasn’t. The gist of the matter is this ;
The bank-clerk “ expert ” merely gives his
opinion as to whether a supposed forgery is like
or unlike the genuine signature. If the imita-
tion is a good one, he is as often wrong as right.
The lithographer, on the other hand, can tell
with the aid of a magnifying-glass if a signature
has been “dashed oft” or laboriously copied.
In nine cases out of ten forgeries come under
the latter category. Few forgers possess the
rare gift of dashing off a colourable imitation
of a signature. Such a forgery will usually
puzzle the owner of the signature himself, and
there is probably not an expert in the world
who could detect it by a mere inspection of the
handwriting. Most of the Maoriland Judges,
it may be added, call for lithographic artists as
experts in forgery cases when their services can
be obtained.

The latest election manifesto of the N.S. Wales
National Ass is an interesting document. It is
full of all the white-haired drivel which has
done duty on so many previous occasions, and
nearly every solemn wdieeze in the Tory vocabu-
lary is there represented. The National Mule
calls for “ sound progressive legislation,” and
the removal from Parliament of * “ men of
shallow capacity ” evidently meaning the
Democratic party —who have created “ general
want of confidence” and caused “capital to be
withdrawn from investment.” Also, it calls
for the return of men with “ high intellectual
and business capacity,” and “upright, able,
thinking men,” and it wants to have payment
of members abolished. Good old Ass ! Its
manifesto sounds like a bumble-bee droning in
a tank, or the sound of the bag-pipes in the far
distance. It has nothing definite to pro-
pose save the election of men of great
stomach-circumference who haven’t seen their
own feet for many years past. It wants white-
hatted men with short legs —men who exude
oil in the hot weather and mop up their smok-
ing heads with large silk handkerchiefs, and
who sleep in their talk and have a solid stake
in the country. Toryism pins its faith to the
short, elderly man with large drops of perspir-
ation standing out on his bald head. If it could
raise a generation of thin Conservatives there
might be some hope for it, for the thin man
looks progressive and the people are deceived
thereby, but the Unnatural Ass distrusts the
thin man, and the country distrusts the fat
man, and so there is a general deadlock.
A new horror has been added to Australian
whisky. The liquor sold by a Sydney
“ hotel ”-keeper was so “strong” that
the gas blew the cork out of the sample
flask, and paper and straw became charred
when dipped into certain ingredients re-
covered by the analyst from the flowing
bowl. What becomes of the drinker’s
stomach when it is acted upon by
such ingredients is a matter for deep
thought. Also, when the whisky blows the
cork out of a flask there is always the possi-
bility that it might blow something out of
the customer. Further, it is an admitted
fact that prohibition leads to secret drink-
ing and the sale of inferior grog, and if N.S.
Wales has got down to sulphuric acid, and so on,
without prohibition—well, there is room for
much foreboding as to the future. When
licensed whisky, sold under State supervision,
blows the cork out, the other kind will probably
lift the roof off and wreck the entire street. In
this case the publican was fined £2O and costs,
which seems a very feeble proceeding. The
man who sells explosive liquor should be classed
with the man who throws explosive bombs
about, and be treated accordingly. Also, it
would be good to enact that when a publican or
other tradesman is convicted of adulteration, he
should be compelled, as in certain parts of
continental Europe, to affix to his front doors
for a month or two the legend in flaring letters:
“John Smith, of this establishment, was fined
£2O for poisoning the public, and ordered to
exhibit on his door for 30 days this record of
his infamy,”
That “one-count” verdict for the plaintiff in
the Speight v. Syme case was objected to on
the high legal ground that no verdicts could be
accepted by the Court during January. Lawyers
armed with Acts and bundles of Rules main-
tained that Justice must necessarily lie torpid
at certain times of the year, especially January,
and that, although a jury might give a verdict
and a Judge might accept it, there was no such
Judge or jury existing at such period within
the pure and beautiful knowledge of the Law.
How honourable the institution that breeds
such arguments ! Strange to say, the objection
did not hold good. An objection tending to
new trials and more expenses is very seldom
dismissed, but in this instance the Law did not
convict itself of doing something unlawful,
as in the Davies grand-jury quash. Two
Judges discovered, after some little trouble,
that they are entitled to sit “every day
of the year and all night long ” if the
emergencies of public business require it. Fixed
vacations are, as a rule, expressions of judicial
contempt for public business. Important suits
are hung up, to the great mental and financial
distress of litigants, in order that Judges may
go fishing, and the “usual adjournment over
Saturday ” is, in at least one case out of five, a
scandalous shame. Why is the legal working
week fixed at 30 hours, less five adjournments
for luncheon ? Because the custom-ridden
public submit to be swindled without consider-
ing where the swindle comes in. The shorter
the day, the more frequent the barristers’
“ refreshers,” the longer the lawyer’s bill, the
greater the chance of the Court rising early,
in order to start fresh next morning,
and so on. When will the public learn
that the law is their servant, not their
master : that it has no authority for the most
of its oppressive arrangements, and that
the first duty of a law-abiding citizen is to
demand a decent, honest exercising of the
power he has agreed to sanction ? Why do we
put an enemy into our Law Courts to steal
away our money ? Where is a Society for the
Suppression of Legal Abuses ? Why does the
average respectable dullard have such an
obtrusive Respect for the Law, the while that
he possesses enough sense to warn his friends
not to go to law on any account? ’Tis a
question which, if printed in tract form and
distributed throughout the country, might
possibly set a few million fools thinking.
It is cabled that “the proprietors of the Salford
Ironworks are so satisfied with the results of
one year’s experiment with the eight-hour
system for the workmen that they intend to
make the system a permanent one. The Times
urges the whole of the engineering and other
skilled trades to adopt eight hours.” Here is
an extract from the report of the Chemical
Works British Parliamentary Committee of
Inquiry (Oct. 1893) :
Attention is called to the long hours of work in some
departments of alkali works, and the adoption of the
eight hour shift is strongly advised. At the works of
Brunner, Mond & Co. (one of the largest, if not the largest,
in Britain), this system has been in force for some time,
and has resulted in a great improvement in the condition
of the health of the men employed. In 1889, before the
eight hour shifts were introduced, 10T2 per cent, of the
employes were in the doctor’s hands; in 1891, only 54
per cent, had to be attended. Brunner, Mond & Co. add
that the cost of wages paid per ton of alkali produced by
them is now no more than it was four years ago, in spite
of the fact that the men employed on shift work are paid
the same wages for eight hours as they were formerly
paid for twelve. This improvement they attribute partly
to the improvement in the apparatus used and partly to
the increased efficiency of the men due to their better
health and spirits.

“ It happens,” says S. M. Herald, “that New
South Wales is the only province that does not
separate the intention from the act of murder,
and it may be hoped that the fate of these two
dangerous men (burglars Montgomery and
Williams) will carry a wholesome lesson to
their fellows, that New South Wales is the last
country to be visited by anyone who thinks of
making a livelihood by violence." First, it is
wrong to say “it happens.” The fact referred
to is one of design. The question was discussed
and re-discussed, and half-a-dozen Hansards are
full of the arguments for and against. Secondly,
the phrase “ intent to murder” is a mere legal
dictionary expression, not indicating that the
party concerned intended to murder, but that
he committed an act which code
takes the liberty to construe into intent.
Thirdly, when the Herald, smacking its lips,
hopes these sentences to death will prevent
other violent people from coming to New South
Wales, it ought, logically, to advocate the
death-penalty against all manner of evil doing.
Then all Australia would be like the S.M.
Herald office.

“US-TRALIA.”
(An ALLEGDRY FOR PERSONAL USE.)
On a section, God-forsaken and forgotten,
Some half-a-dozen cart-loads of humanity were dumped,
Foetid, tainted to the core, diseased and rotten—
In one naked load of nastiness, all specimens were
lumped!
They were left to reek and rot,
For the carters cared no jot
If they blasted all the section with their blight 1
Xho’ there breeded worm with worm
Till the mass began to squirm,
For the present they had put them out of sight!
But soon upon the muck-heap grass was springing,
And curtaining with verdure all the festering, foetid
place !
Its arms to sunnier skies more freely flinging,
Manured by muck arose a young and self-reliant race !
And that race will grow and grow,
And let the nations know
How that putrid and polluted may be pure;
And declare to all, although
Tis ’now buried far below.
There was stamina and strength in that manure .

“ the sun ad set behind the lonley hills
and the day ad closed once more
the night owels call loud and shrill
vviist the dingowes owel in scores
neath a seder tree wee lay
in blankets that are wet and cold
wating for the coming day
to clime those mountains boald
wee have dimed ore stoney ridges
and gaised on loftey watter falls
wee have crosed creeaks without bridges
that the bravest would apall
but now amongst the leaches
wilst the fire is birning low
we lay with paled feature
that once were all a glowe
and wilst I ham a scrawling
trying to dot those virces down
close by a snake is crawling
for oure camp fire he as found
but let us have a smoak
and pass the billey round
we can easley stand a joak
a patch of seader wee ave found.”

van drawn by a good-looking horse drove up and
down, placarded on either side: “ This complete
turn-out, horse, van, and driver, ill a week and
tucker.”

railway-syndicates fight shy of MTlwraith on the
question of survey expenses. This is Nemesis
with a vengeance. Evidently the fate of Feild-
ing’s ’Bl transcontinental syndicate has been
marked and digested. The MTlwraith Govt,

but before the cash was paid Griffith got in and
wouldn’t hand over a cent. The repudiation of
’Bl has more than a little to do with the difficulties
of ’94, Money has a long memory.
