My Dear Moorabinda—
Mayoress Manning’s hugely-attended reception
in the Tovyn Hall on the dim date of Tuesday
before last is now nearly forgotten by the crowds
of people who fought pitched battles at the
buffets, tho’ possibly the woman who broke her
parasol in the pursuit of pastry, thereby paying
at the rate of ten and sixpence for a sausage-roll,
has still lively recollections of what footballers call
the “scrum.”
1 have now but the faintest notion of what any*
body wore, but there seemed to be fewer back*
numbers present than usual, and about one man
appeared to the millinery mile. The hostess, who
received at the main entrance to the beautifully-
decorated hall, wore an English-looking visiting-
dress of seal-brown satin cloth, velvet and fur,
with bonnet to match, which became her to a T,
as her favourite colour should; the London
JOURNAB-looking Mayor also lent a hand in a very
actual sense, and Miss Felicia Manning, in teru,
contrasted with claret-coloured velvet, helped to
reassure a lot of people who came in looking as if
“beyond lay the Star Chamber.”
A new idea at this reception was that the big
hall was strewn with chairs, which, however
acceptable to the aged, made the affair as dismal
as an afternoon organ-recital, and rather broke
the back of sociability at first, A mildly-funny
incident was when a little fuss of people paraded
in at the doorway and Wiegand, at the organ,
struck up into the “Faust” music, a number of
people started to their feet, mistaking it for the
National Anthem heralding the Governor ! But
the most awful part of that little error was that
anybody could mistake even half a bar of Gounod’s
masterpiece for “Godsave.”
X hear that at a fashionable resort, last summer,
His Excellency of Barataria committed a very
dismal blunder. A ball was given, and the
hostesses of the evening—all leading society
women—received the vice-regal party with open
arms and posed ’em—l mean the party—on the
dais ; but guess the surprise of the top-creamy
assemblage when, before eleven o’clock, the V.R.
posse swept out of the hall without one word of
adieu to their entertainers, in stiff retreat, as if
her ladyship had caught some pretty woman
winking at the Gov. in a manner contrary to the
Act in that case made and provided.
At the first opportunity the chief hostess
politely demanded a reason for the sudden vice-
regal withdrawal before supper (which, en paren-
thfcse, had been especially well-put-up in honour of
the gubernatorial presence). Imagine her sur-
prise when she was told, “Oh, we left because we
didn’t think there was going to be any supper!
If we had known, of course ” Future com-
mittees are advised to include the information
that supper will be on the tables at 11 p.rn. sharp,
if they want to keep His Ex. trimming the dais
after ten-thirty.
After all, there is a great deal in a name, and if
Strauss had christened his “ Blue Danube” waltz
“Homage to Cooma,” as Miss Mary Noonan
names a waltz she forwards me, I don’t think
even that exquisite melody could have caught on.
Though, as a matter of plain truth, if the “Homage
to Cooma” waltz had been christened “Love’s
Loudest Hug ” it wouldn’t have helped it much,
for it is a weak-knee’d jingle not always in
harmony, and with only one movement —the third
—in which there is any approach to rhythm.
.Another lady sends a “Cecilia Gavotte,”
dedicated to her mother —probably because she is
the only person who wouldn’t hit back—and the
list of home-grown music closes with a Reverie
and Berceuse by Daisy Cfaamier. I like both
these morsels —especially the cradle-song, which is
melodious and reposeful, the change of scale from
one to five sharps and vice-versa being very
smoothly managed. The only mistake in the
Reverie is the name. In music people don’t dream
in gavotte time to “ vivace ” sign-marks.
People who grinned at the notion of the Maori-
laud Women’s Rights wedding, at which, it was
widely alleged, the bride wore knickerbockers, will
probably regret to hear that she did nothing of the
sort. A woman who had the muoh-talked-of
welding garment in her hand tells me that it was
simply the old familiar divided-skirt, which much
resembles the ord nary skirt unless the wearer
happens to be stepping out like the party in seven-
league boots, or posing in a high wind —and that
the scene at the altar was as humdrum as possible.
Between “Lady HarVrton’s ” and brazen bifur-
cations there is a great gulf fixed, and it is pleasant
to think that the Maoriland bridegroom may yet
await his little bride without fearing the
Ih * audience may have to look at her through its
fingers.
It is five years since the busy B.’s staged
“ Dandy Dick ” in Sydney, so many points of that
broad and racy comedy came freshly to the over-
flowing audience at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
evening. They don’t dress “Dandy Dick ” as
heretofore, although Mrs. Brough, as the turfy
George Tid, wore the identical racy rig in the first
act, but which somehow didn’t look so sporting as
of yore. Since first Mrs. B. cocked her Derby
hat rakishly at a Sydney audience the Block has
rendered unto us even more weirdly-mannish
ladies than the part-owner of Dandy Dick;
although Mrs. Brough has her checkered vest out
straight without fitting, a realistic touch you never
find in the most inexorably tailor-made girl in
real life. The average woman in a cut-away coat
never looks convincing while she belts herself
into 19 inches, and a damsel whose coated and
vested trunk infers that she wears a skirt under
protest, but who yet clings to the hour-glass out-
line, is as hopelessly feminine as something in
frills “from neck to knee.” George Tid’s slops
look as if she really meant it.
In the second act, Mrs. Brough, in a fiery-hued
silk, didn’t swamp old memories of a grey and
saffron confection, like jonquills in a mist, that she
was wont to wear, but her race toilet of neatly-
filled tobacco-brown fitted as slimly as ever.
Emma Temple, in a neatly-fitting tackle, took
Florence Harcourt’s part as Salome, and looked
more like a Dean’s daughter than the fluffy Flor-
ence, who ran to pale-blue tea-gowns and only
wanted a cigarette to look like the female villain ;
and ohirrupy Brenda Gibson played the toy child,
in pink with starfish bows of black velvet, with
all her old elfishness.
But few of the men are dandies in “Dandy
Dick.” Titheradge (I think he makes a more
hypocritical Dean than Anson), in episcopal
gaiters, suggested that he had bandaged his legs
on the mummy principle, and Mr. Carey’s
Baronet looked like a book-agent. Cecil Ward
and Chichester displayed much millinery as the
young officers (one of whom Emma Temple calls
“ lef-£en-ant,” by the way), but let no man laugh
at them in their weird fancy dress, for they are as
representative a pair of society youths disguised
in the garb of forgotten monarchs, as you need
sample. I have never seen a man yet who could
carry off a pair of lace-edged knickerbockers with-
out looking like a Chinese scarecrow, and the only
thing which makes me doubtful of the success of a
handsome Sydney society lawyer who is going on
the stage is that I once saw him in red velveteen
as some wicked duke or other at a club ball, and
haven’t forgotten the spectacle yet.
The position of the man whose wife finds a
golden hair on his coat after a “club-night,” is
joy itself compared to that of the Sydney fashion-
able dancing-master who went into court to defend
himself on a charge of slander, brought by a
married lady, whom he had accused of frisky
backslidings from the paths of conventional pro-
priety while pirouetting in his halls of dazzling
light.
The awkward part of the dancing-man’s posi-
tion was that, to gain his case, he had to make it
appear that his academy was a place where dancers
weren’t shattered by hiccoughs in the mazy waltz
as a general thing, nor did any lady ever retire into
a shadowy corner to be hugged between the dances.
But yet—to show up the alleged frisky frivollings of
the plaintiff—he had to do his level best to prove that
it was possible to be intoxicated, and exceedingly
amorous, on his bees-waxed premises if you felt so
inclined, without being run out on the pavement
—which is not a nice impression to get about with
regard to a fashionable dancing academy.
The jury assessed the damages at .£3OO for the
plaintiff, though, when you come to think of all
that was said in court, it strikes you that neither
party gets very much change out of the trouble.
Temperance hinders a multitude of winks. It
amused me to read the other day that a gentle-
man “and his wife” had presented to Miss
Ackermann, the tea-apostle, a cheque for a thou-
sand pounds. “ And wife ” makes all the differ-
ence, of course, though I suppose the wife’s part
consists mainly in lending her name for publica-
tion, as a guarantee of good faith. Women, you
see, rarely give one another anything but hypo-
critical kisses and fancy-work, but I can’t help
appreciating a calling which enables a spinster to
take a thumping cheque from a man (and wife) to
purchase a home in her old age. Miss Ackermann,
I see, has generously given the <£looo to a fund
for establishing a head-quarters in Sydney for the
W.C.T.U.
However, the sinners get testimonials as well as
the saints. When a barmaid at a fashionable
Sydney hotel left, the other day, a testimonial of
about 200 sovereigns was got up amongst the
habitues as a mark of respect and esteem. Where
is the woman who would not rather thus receive a
sovereign apiece from 200 men than a cool thou-
sand from one (and wife) ?
It strikes me that a most effective way to abolish
barmaids, and, in fact, to utterly close the bars,
would be for all the ogresses of the W.C.T.U. to
turn Hebes and preach sermons over the counter
to thirsty unregenerates. No man would deny
the abominations of the barmaid system who had
ever been received by a W.C.T.IJ. tabby in his
favourite hotel. Then there would be no vague
metaphor about “ the horrors of drink.”
To most women the strongest indictment against
barmaids is that most men will have it they are
• good and charming creatures. Man’s praise is
always balanced by woman’s blame. If Mr. Dash
told Mrs. Dash that Tottie, who has all the other
fellows raving, ought to come to her for a wrinkle
in frizzing her front hair, I’d bet a banana that
Mrs. Dash would walk slowly past that pub. so
that Tottie might view the masterpiece ! It is
men’s hot championship of Tottie that has given
rise to this fierce feminine longing to abolish her.
In the case for wife desertion brought against
Christie Murray, in London, the novelist admitted
living at Kensington with a girl who had left
Melbourne in his company. I am told that the
girl’s family were so sure of her hymeneal status
that, on the occasion of an addition to Mr. Mur-
ray’s new family last year, they had the event
duly advertised in both the Melbourne papers.
That “ wife of Christie Murray ” won a musical
scholarship in Victoria, and her feet and their
motion were as dainty as those immortalised in
Suckling’s verses.
A certain Australian Judge, on a visit to another
province, once advertised persistently for a gover.
ness, and held out such high terms and rosy pros.
peots that numerous charming girls, whose people
had come down in the world, made application.
The Judge’s advt. may have been but the ruse of
a gallant visitor desirous of feasting his eyes on
the local beauties, or it may have been a legitimate
effort to study humanity; anyhow, one of the
applicants, a very pretty girl indeed, after being
fooled to a certain extent, spoke out her mind.
“Your Honor,” said she, coyly, to the delightful
old family man, “I’m afraid I won’t do. I only
want to be a governess; what you need is evi-
dently a Judge’s associate.”
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, April 10, 1894.
Talking of eyes reminds me that Lalla Miranda’s
departure is drawing very near. She farewelled
at the Town Hall on Saturday. Musical wise-
acres declare that the migration of this pretty
song-bird has been too long postponed, that her
voice is gone already, her high notes have degene-
rated into shrieks, and that her physique —she
looks not a bit more fragile than Adelina Patti —
was never made to stand the wear and tear of
systematic training. Ah, well! Time will show,
and, at all events, Lalla will ever have much to be
thankful for in the face she uses so cleverly.
Ada Crossley was not nearly so fortunate in this
respect, and when she first came out, and before
she had acquired her happy knack of successful
dressing, the contralto used to look most prosaic
as she stood singing straight ahead, in a matter-
of-fact sort of way. Her parents are not romantic
either, I believe, though they dwell in the depths
of Gippsland, where they “run” a “bush inn.”
The family boasts a younger daughter, whose
singing voice is said to be even more promising
than that of her popular sister. We badly need a
few such “ finds ” to console us for the loss of
departing favourites.
Vera Goad warbles tunefully, but she is married,
and, in consequence (though this rule has many
skittish exceptions) dispenses with ornamental
airs and graces; while (Sara Lewis, who is also
joined in the bonds, is said to find her lack of
musical ear a sad drawback to the success of her
really fine voice.
There has been quite a boom of uppercrust
engagements lately. No woman relishes the idea
of being the last rose of summer, so when one
bud is swept into the vortex of matrimony, the
rest of the cluster generally do their best to
follow. The last names sent in for “announce-
ment ” are those of Miss Simson and Mr. Ernest
De Little.
The Hopetouns spent part of their late bush
holiday at Caramut, Mr. De Little’s country
home. The visit seems to have been attended
with good results, as not so very long ago their host
was supposed to be “wearing the willow.” In
any case that sort of thing soon palls on human
nature.
It was at Caramut that Lady Hopetoun made
her most recent canine purchase. Getting up
early one morning she sallied forth with her gun
“in a homely, even jolly way ” (see Argus account
of the Governor’s driving tour), and feeling hungry
en route shot some duck for breakfast. The birds
fell into the water, and, notwithstanding her appe-
tite, the Countess didn’t feel inclined to earn her
repast by a swim. Just then she caught sight of
a rabbiter’s hut and soon made her way to its
lowly portal, where, rousing the inmates from their
slumbers, she requested them with characteristic
bonhomie to lend her their dawg. The animal
proving equal to the occasion, landed the game in
first-class style, and found so much favour in the
sight of his patroness that she bought him from his
sordid owner for the sum of £7, and led him
away to more high-toned surroundings, where he
is already learning to flash his ivories at the
proletariat.
A host, whose domestic staff comprises only a
“plain” or family cook and a combination house
and parlour maid, might be expected to quail at
the very idea of a lengthy vice-regal visitation. A
young squatter, so circumstanced, who, with his
sister, lately entertained a Government House
party, is said to have sustained the ordeal with
the haughty aplomb of a hundred-vassaled Lord.
The leading lady of the occasion, being fond of
cold baths, made a practice of changing every
item of her apparel whenever she came in from a‘
hunting, shooting, or fishing expedition, which
sometimes happened three or four times a day.
One can imagine that the laundry presented a
scene of incessant activity during her visit, but as
both she and her inferior half were ready with
“tips” and displayed no weaknesses in the
matter of cookery, except one harmless little
passion for scones, Bridget and Emma are not
drifting into anarchism.
As seen at recent concerts, Charles Saunders
still boasts an amiably adipose aspect. I read
somewhere, the other day, that “Fat men are not
forceful.” Perhaps not, but they are good-
tempered, which is more to the point, when one
considers the ordeals by button and collar, the
conflicts with starch and stud, which beset male
human nature. Caesar showed his sense in saying
“Let me have men about me that are fat,”
because he would naturally prefer to sit with
Romans who laughed at his jokes as though they
were really funny. Even if the plump person be
stupid, so much the better; geniuses, as Mrs.
Carlyle proclaimed for the warning and instruction
of her sex, are not domestic sunbeams. There are
too few of the species (Geniuses, I mean) in Melb.
to point my moral, but the only one who really
acts up to the character became a grass-widower
after half a year’s matrimony, and he is as thin as
a whipping-post.
To return to Mr. Saunders, people say he is as
good-natured as he looks. Certainly, the other
night, he gave one the impression of unalloyed
benevolence as he stood talking to two pretty girls
in the front chairs, though that goes for little.
Charles’s tenor songs must be a liberal education
in the art of looking sweet. He is used to
“ taking ” a pair of sparkling eyes, and to imper-
sonating a pilgrim of love who hasn’t reduced his
girth by walking.
At a Wangaratta concert, some time back, th e
plump tenor won golden opinions by his obliging
conduct in reeling all his songs straight off one
after another, when, owing to an unexpected
summons to Sydney, he had to leave early to catch
his train. The audience was insatiable and en-
cored him every time. At last he tore himself
away, and the girls were just beginning to feel
sad when *he suddenly re-appeared and an-
nounced beamingly, “I find I have four minutes
to spare, sol’ll sing you one more song.” (Tre-
mendous enthusiasm.)
Miss Manifold’s marriage with Mr. Fyfe-
Jamieson, postponed on account of the decease of
the bride’s mother, came off quietly one day this
week at Camperdown. If things had turned out
differently, the wedding of the last of the Mani-
fold girls was to have been an affair of surpassing
magnificence, with the Earl and Countess starred
on the invitation list. The Vice-regal couple,
during their recent tour, stayed with: one of the
clan, and on that occasion Lady Hopetoun pre-
sented Miss Manifold with a four-in-hand.
A tew days afterwards the distinguished donor
was tooling this turnout to the Camperdown races
when the horses took fright, plunged, kicked up
and broke the splashboard. Their driver was
quite cool, and soon reduced them to order. She
seems to have no fear of horses, though notori-
ously shy of mayoresses, as the Geelong people
know to their cost.
The first time her ladyship visited the seaside
town, she wore her arm in a sling as the result of
a painful encounter with a falling blind-roller.
Some of the citizens (or was it their wives?)
steeled their hearts against this explanation and
regarded the bandage as a diabolical ruse for
dodging the handshakes of leal and loyal Gee-
longeese. Years rolled on and Time’s effacing
finger had dealt kindly with this grievance when,
about four months ago, Gov. Hopetoun was
invited to open the Geelong Channel and to bring
with him his now forgiven spouse. The local
mayoress, at that time, was a lady of meek and
quiet spirit who didn’t relish the idea of being
paired off with the august Countess any more
than that imposing personage hankered after
the society of her official guide. However, both
ladies had to go through with the affair and
they passed a most trying day from 10a.m. to 6p.m.
in each other’s company, during which period
they hardly exchanged a remark, or ‘‘swopped ” a
smile. In the meantime, the Governor was posi-
tively overflowing with geniality, and at the end
of his long bout of gush he stood on the platform
of the Melbourne train and gazed regretfully upon
the beauties of Geelong, as if loath to leave.
Just then a horny-handed son of toil appeared,
and, pushing his way through the crowd, intro-
duced himself as a humble admirer of the Hieland
chieftain. The latter responded rapturously, and
a most affecting scene ensued, Lady Hopetoun,
even, being inveigled from the inner, fastnesses of
the carriage to pat the devoted interloper on the
back. At last, the train steamed off with its pre-
cious freight, the wily noble protesting to the last
how intensely charmed he was with his new
acquaintance.
They do say that a distinguished somebody even
injured her ankle occasionally rather than dance
in the same set with one of the local high-fliers.
Fortunately a “set” contains several people, there-
fore no one could be certain who was the culprit.
Besides, if those Geelong women were right in
their surmises about the “ bruised arm,” a limb-
complaint may be instituted on very slight
grounds.
Ercildoune, except when putting on a spurt to
welcome the few congenial spirits which haunt
this democratic country, is sadly shorn of its old-
time glories. Two or three maids trip indus-
triously abjut, as if the place was a suburban
villa, while the sons of the house are called up at
dawn by their merciless papa and kept hard at it
all day, clad in ordinary “ bushman’s costume.”
No doubt Salmon finds titled children-in-law
are expensive luxuries. Lady Sarah Wilson,
though, was very popular with the employes
on the place, who compare her unaffected
demeanour with the airs and graces of some of her
marriage connections, and unanimously vote her
“ the best of the bunch.” Ercildoune will soon be
left to the tender mercies of the two younger boys,
as the head of the house doesn’t contemplate
wasting the s weetness of himself and daughters on
Australian air much longer. So he is off to chase
the coveted, but coy, baronetcy in the neighbour-
hood of Piccadilly.
The engagement of Victoria’s chief heiress||is
announced at last. The wonder is that she hasn’t
been snapped up long ago, but naturally the
peerage idea wasn’t resigned without a struggle.
No one would have given the old uncle, who en-
couraged this ambition by a legacy (which, I
believe, the young lady gets in any case), credit for
so much enterprise. He himself, once in a
moment of excitement, plighted his troth to a
member of the check-apron sisterhood. Hearing
that the contemplation of his wealth had so
inflated the ideas of the bride-elect that she
refused to have a handkerchief in her trousseau
costing less than <£l sterling, he broke off the
match with a snap.
One of our richest squatting crowds is said to be
engaged at the present moment in the autumn
pastime of starving the local pastor out. In what
way the unhappy apostle has incurred their wrath
has not been divulged, but, so far, he stands firm
and talks largely about “Death and Imprison-
ment.” Somebody in authority, pitying the
weaker side, called a meeting in the hope of recon-
ciling the contending parties. But “ the family ”
ignored the peacemakers and declined all over-
tures, an easy position to take up when you
have inherited the earth and the other fellow is
merely a poor gospel-miller with 12 children, more
or less, to devour his thick bread and butter.

£lh^:::::::::: variety show.
“ Dandy Dick,” staged at Sydney Lyceum
on Saturday, is not a piece of the calibre of
“ Niobe; ” nevertheless, it is a wholesome and
pleasant comedy which appeals to the laughter-
loving sinner. The principal character m it is a
horse. This artist never appears personally on the
stage but his presence is felt from the middle of
the first act right to the end. The all-pervading-
ness of that horse, his magnetic affinity, the soul-
fulness of him, and his capacity for moulding
events is something remarkable. He enters un-
invited into a quiet clerical family, and inside of
24 hours he reduces it to gibbering insanity. By
his evil influence the Dean’s two daughters take
to betting and staying out late at night; the Dean
himself stakes the collection money on a race and
mixes up horse medicines secretly at the dead
of • night ; and the old family retainer
attempts a hideous crime with strychnine.
Then the cleric is arrested for “nobbling, ’ and
is further charged with attempted seduction of a
policeman’s wife—said lady being an ex-cook.
Further, his widowed sister harangues a crowd of
jockeys, spielers, welshers, bookmakers and horse-
copers on a racecourse, and shares in a free fight,
and finally the abandoned dignitary is brought
home insensible, disgraced, and fallen from his
high estate, with a nose-bag over his head, to find
that the collection money has been inadvertently
staked on the wrong animal. Inside of a day and
a night a whole household is devastated and laid
in ruins by one horse, yet the malign beast him-
self except that his tail is burned, remains abso-
lutely calm and uninjured amid the general
downfall. # #
Mrs. Brough, Titheradge and Boucicault
are the main supports of this horse-story. Tither-
adge, as an unotuous and short-winded Dean of
a large, helpless pattern, is reasonably good. It
is not very clear at any time whether he means his
ecclesiastic to be a deceptive snuffler or a good
man gone wrong by accident, but as the average
dean never quite knows which he is himself this
doesn’t signify. Probably Titheradge intends the
audience to take its choice. Mrs. Brough,
however, is sufficiently definite. She- is the
wildest, horsiest, doggiest lady on record, and
her occasional hardness of manner makes the
horsiness and the dogginess suit her to adrnira-
tife" As for Boucicault’s ancient retainer—-who
is old Jaikes gone hopelessly to the bad—he is the
best of the trio. A red-headed policeman played
by Mr. Brough—is also entirely commendable,
and is an interesting essay on the force in general;
and the rest of the eo. looks beautiful, and wears
its new frock w v ith excellent effect. Next week
the great play of the season, “ The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray,” will be produced, and local society
will totter on its foundations —or, at least, it ought
to.
* * » *
“ The Lights of London,” a piece which
happens to everybody sooner or later, recurred
at Sydney Her Majesty’s on Saturday night. It
got a large, warm reception—in fact, the house
was almost as enthusiastic as if it had never seen
the “Lights” before. The company is in good
working order, the scenery is gorgeous, and the
tank is as successful as ever in its own quiet way.
The tank and Mr. Rignold’s dive from the bridge
are the great features of the show, and the gallery
vociferates just as it used to do when the water
splashes up some fourteen or fifteen feet, or there-
abouts, as the monarch falls in. Then it tries to
bring Henry V. before the curtain in the hope
that when he bows the damp after-part of him
will leave a moist imprint on the drop-scene as a
guarantee of good faith. Miss Kate Bishop
makes a valuable addition to the co. and there is
a new policeman in place of the departed Sweeney.
The remainder of the cast does its duty well m
that station of life to which has called
it, and the villain will begin his nefarious career
at 7.45 precisely until further notice.
. Last week The Bulletin took occasion to
remark that it might be an appropriate ciroum-
stance if a cathedral fell upon “Cinders, at
Sydney Royal, and blotted it out. The remark
was made in haste, and has since been repented at
leisure, on the ground that it would be a sinful
waste of a good cathedral. “Hans the Boatman
has packed up the kiddies and the big dog and
gone to Adelaide, and the Criterion is temporarily
deserted. A few minor shows are bearing up
pretty well against the damp hand of calamity,
but the damp hand of calamity, as a rule, has the
best of it.
* * * *
George Larmour, proprietor of Ooogee
Aquarium, intends to fight the antiquated law
and the fossilised sentiment which prohibit Sunday
concerts. The Bulletin sincerely hopes that
men and women who believe in freedom of thought
and action will back him up, by patronising the
concerts as long as they are decently conducted
and give value for the money. The attempt to
make Sunday a day of gloom and horror is opposed
both to sound morality and common-sense.
* * * *
Fillis’s Circus has now got the additional
support of Benham’s learned pig, an animal of
varied knowledge and deep philosophical research.
“Deadwood Dick,” a wild, hair-raising tragedy
w-ith much shooting in it, and much tearing around
of half-breeds, cowboys, and hairy desperadoes,
and with many corpses left to be eaten by vultures
on the plain, is the other principal addition to the
programme. Nobody knows exactly what all the
shooting is about, but it is a pleasant and horrify-
ing episode, and is good value for the money.
# * * *
The comedy at Sydney Bijou is now finish-
ing its third and last week, and is still going
tolerably strong. The “Strategist” is a comedy
which ought to be good for several more weeks,
especially with so excellent a company, but “ the
depression” is against it, as it is against every-
thing else. Miss Gladys Leigh is brightening up
its last days with some new songs, and The Bul-
letin is pieased to mention that she has a pleasant
voice and is a lady with whom anyone would be
§ lad to elope at the shortest notice. “Hide and
eek’’ and “ Mixed ” are in preparation.
* * * *
Out of compliment to Courtneidge the
played “La Mascotte,” at Melbourne Princess’s,
last Friday night, and in the baldhead of Farmer
Rocco.that incisive comedian raised his farewell
laugh. During the show Courtneidge was so
noisily called for that he had to slip Ryley s coat
over his.demi-toilet and burst upon the scene with
a promise to make a speech in due course. Which
oration, being neat and simple, helped to atone
for the few words that have come from several
previous professional funny men on similar
occasions.
#* * *
On Saturday a revival of “La Cigale”
satisfied a still shuddering world that Miss Violet
Varley was neither dead nor sleeping. Earlier in
the week, as everybody knows, the newspapers
had sung of her rescue from the jaws of decease.
It is only a simple country ditty, to the air of
“ One day Marg6t ” :
One day Y.V.,
Wife soon to be,
Tumbled right into a lift-well.
Oh-o-o, the cruel lift-well, cru-el lift-well.
Poor promised bride
There might have died.
Crushed out of knowledge when the lift fell,
“ Oh, dear me!” cried Miss Y.Y.,
“’Tis coming, see! ’tis coming, see-ee !”
Bub her peril quite unique was averted by a
shriek
The cage was stopp’t in time, whilst she scrambled
fro-o-om the lift- well.
So, you see, pretty V
didn’t dee—
She did not dee.
Oh-o-o-o, Oh, pretty maiden ;
No-no-no-no, No, pretty maiden,
You were not jammed after all, although you
might have been.
So-so-so-so, So, pretty maiden,
All the air with joy is laden ;
You are wed, instead of dead —God save the
Queen !
It was understood that Miss Varley will assume
the character of Mr. Tapley’s rib pn Wednesday
of this current week, wherefore criticism of their
respective performances in “La Cigale ” is
graciously withheld. To stand in the shadow of
death upon the awful brink of matrimony is a
trying experience for anybody, and although
Tapley didn’t personally tumble under the lift, his
voice took a half-share of the nervous shock, all
the same. .“Princess Ida,” with a recuperated
Nellie Stewart in the name part, is to follow three
nights of the other opera and bring the present
season to a finish.
** * *
On Saturday the Bland Holt Dramatic
Company will appear at the Princess’ in “ A
Woman’s Revenge,” a play that has been tested
and declared A 1 in the “provinces.” A strong
company includes Miss Watson, Sass and Edith
Blande, and the accessories are said.to be Bland
Holtian in the extreme.
** * *
The Bijou re-opens for one last farewell
week of “ Charley’s Aunt,” on April 21st. Arthur
Garner, at the head of a syndicate, will take pos-
session of the house in May, and start with a
comedy on the 7th of that merrie month. All the
poetry of May being antipodean, it is proper to
assume that our trees will begin to bud when
Garner raises the curtain.
** m *
Lizzie Stofel gathers in the ducats as
before at Melbourne Royal. Judging from
present appearances “Struck Oil” might run
right along into the dim future, and hoary parents
as yet unborn might expect to hear that crash of
crockery in “ The Chinese Question. But it is
possible that Maggie Moore will want to change
the bill before then.
* * *
“ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” pursues its dusky,
dislocated way at the Alexandra, and the
sympathetic stout female, who pays a shil-
ling to drop a tear for little Eva, is
taking her grief steadily. Little Eva is a
festive, buxom little damsel, with legs that seem
to have destined her for_ the ballet. Her
passing away is, therefore, inexplicable, but if
Uncle Tom were to throw in a casual announce-
ment that the angel-child had been mortally
wounded by a tram, she could die just as nicely,
and nobody need wonder at her apotheosis.
Under present conditions no doctor would write
a certificate until he had held a post-mortem ex-
amination of the infant’s elegantly-rounded corse,
and then he would have to give a Latin guess at
the cause of death. Very likely she succumbs to
the part-singing of her papa’s niggers. “Uncle
Tom ” is likely to last a few nights longer, another
coloured drama, Boucioault’s “ Octoroon, being
next on the Alexandra list.
At Poverty Point.
John Stool's blood-stained head-bandage runs “Tosca”
close for realism. Johnny Forde has been fche cause of
sending more than one susceptible female, at Melb.
Theatre Royal, into a “dead faint” at the sanguinary
sight.
The apotheosis of Uncle Tom, at Melbourne Alexandra-
is decidedly original. A duplicate of the original nigger,
clad in red shirt and dingy pants, is borne upward by the
angels who previously called for little Eva. It occurred
to the management that a black spirit wouldn’t match
well with a white gown and wings, so Uncle has to go
disguised as a mortal.

piece and another generation has “come on ” since then.
Some’ enterprising British manager might do worse than
give Jennie a fresh turn around.

The three years' engagement of M. Wiegand as Sydney
city organist expires next month. Other local organists
are agitating against a renewal, asserting that no man
should have a monopoly of the big organ. What most or
them individually mean is that no other man should have
a monopoly of it. A lot of them applied for the position
and some, such as Mr. Sykes, while holding that unofficial
organists should have an opportunity of playing public y,
have the decency to speak of M. Wiegand as a talented
brother-artist. Others a tack him in all sorts of cowardly
and underhand ways. The Bulletin is rather surprised
to find that M. Wiegand dares to go out o nights.
People “ in the know,” or who should know, do say that
that romantic theatrical marriage has already taken
place.

Rose Musgrove, second daughter of manager George,
has a burning desire to go on the stage, for which pro-

“"Miss Duvalli, who benefited at Melb. Theatre Royal last
year and went to Europe for vocal study, has returned
weighted with disappointment. Her voice, which once
promised so well, proved unequal to further study, and
she is now obliged to resume, for the present, the family
profession, dancing.
When Fillis’s circus was at Auckland (M.L.) a local ad-
vertising doctor scored a big puff by entering the
cage and there reciting the “Charge of the Light
Brigade.” At least it would have been a big putt
if the medicine-man hadn’t looked so haggard and
his knees hadn’t had a tendency to bang together. But
he escaped the fate he deserved. Any one who recites the
“Charge of the Light Brigade” ought to be eaten by
lions.

counted as a big item in her favour at the Judgment, and
meanwhile it pays a lot better here below than having
her diamonds stolen.
Herr Bandmann, whose unspeakably bad acting has
kept his memory green in this country, has lately received
bad news at his cow-ranch out by Sacramento. His
Montana divorce was annulled, consequently Millicent
Palmer had the doubtful pleasure of being his wife once
more, and the second Mrs. Bandmann was ejected. Then
the first wife obtained a divorce on her own account, and
struck the Herr heavily for law-expenses and alimony,
and the status of the dethroned second wife remains un-
settled.
A son of Alfred Plumpton, the conductor, is acting with
some success at Melb. Alexandra.
The gushful Olga Nethersole to a recent interviewer
“I hope I shall not live to grow old. Ido so long to play
great parts ! Sometimes I feel that I oould play them as
they should be played through the force of their inspira-
tion. I should like to live long enough to fulfil my
ambition—to play the part I yearn for most, to play it, if
only once, as I yearn to play it —and then I could die as
the curtain fell.” The part of a nice, fresh, good-looking
corpse ought to fit Olga like a glove.
Miss Alice Holbrook, of Melbourne, has got it announced
by a cable that she is engaged to take part in a new
Gaiety burlesque. If the part is no bigger than those she
played in Australia there wasn’t any urgent necessity for
a cablegram. The Bulletin remembers Miss Holbrook as
a young lady who was very anxious to get a notice in this
journal. She did get one at last. It was but brief, yet it
must have satisfied Alice, for she never worried to get
another. The charming Alice, though said to be of Melb.,
was a 'Murkan accented damsel, if we remember aright.
A country Press Agency once mentioned her in its little
Melb. letter, from which time forth she haunted the office
of that Agency, enquiring for one or other of the thirty-
five organs in which her paragraph had appeared. _ It was
always the same old paragraph, but Alice liked to indulge
the hollow fancy that 35 different Melb. corres-
pondents had given her a notice. She would speak of the
“ criticism ” in the Budgywugy Chronicle as though it
were quite a different thing from the Qurragarry Banner’s
bit of butter, and quite seriously she used to ask the clerk
at the Agency to show her more copies of papers that had
been “saying something nice” about her.
At present Sims Reeves, who threatens an Australian

for a fee equal to 30s. an hour.
The ballet-master at London Alhambra says that Con-
tinental danseuses extinguish the English girls because
the latter are too lazy to practise. Those eight brides-
maids that George Musgrove brought out here for “ Tur-
quoisette ” had not learned todance much the first instance,
let alone practising afterwards. They were quite Hing-
lish, and the word “ rehearsal ” sickened ’em.
Sarah Bernhardt meets all the household expenses of
her son Maurice, whose wife (a princess with an unpro-
nounceable name) brought him a fortune of £l6OO a year.
This income is, by Sarah’s orders, accumulating for the
future benefit of the happy pair, whilst she pays all their
bills, so that some of her money may not be wasted. She
can’t save money personally, whereas they can save
through her by the present arrangement. The £l6OO a
year Sarah says, must accumulate and multiply until
after her death ; till Men she will defray all their expenses.
Wonderful Sarah ! She sees no time coming when her
charm will fade, or her health fail. She is to go on acting
at £lOO a night, or so, until she drops suddenly into the
grave. It is quite possible, though, that Maurice wonders
what he will do in the event of mama demanding her
usual £lOO to scatter around when she hasn’t a shilling to
bless herself with. He’ll have to tell the raging genius
that his bank is in course of reconstruction.

The shade of Shakespeare, remarks The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, must have had a bad time during
the performance of “As You Like It ” by a co. of ladies,
unassisted by even one of the played-out sex. As an
exhibition of effrontery it has never been equalled, and
as a stage performance it was simply grotesque. Some of
the ladies had fine legs, and obviously took pride in
showing them. King Ja-Ja might have gone farther and
not found so good a lot of recruits for his regiment of
Amazons, and this is all that can be said for them. One
fact is worth recording—the wrestling scene was much less
absurd than when it was played by Larry Foley, in
Sydney.

An Australian theatrical manager was offered, the
other day, a sensational melodrama to which he took only
one objection. The language was too good for his
audiences. There were no blank verse soliloquies or long
sketches of poetical dialogue, and the characters were up
to date, but their grammar frightened the manager. It
is hard to make a theatrical person understand that the
patrons of fustian can always appreciate good material,
as evidenced by the survival of Shakespeare at low-priced
London houses. Leaving the Bard out of the question,
what audience is too uneducated for “ The Silver King ’
or “ Corsican Brothers”—well-worded plays both of them ?

The Wild-Cat Column.
The N.S.W. Government has dropped the tSank
of North Queensland out of the Bank Notes Hill—that
famous measure which makes notes legal tender in all
parts of the province save the metropolis—the reason
being that its Sydney office is the only one which it
possesses in N.S. VV., and consequently it has no need for
a note circulation. The same process of reasoning
applies, in a less degree, to the National Bank and the
B.N.Z., which have each one solitary branch in the
country districts. Also, it applies in its entirety to the
Q.N. Bank, which also has no office at all outside Sydney ;
yet it is included in the I ill, and its notes arc made legal
tender all over the province, and the public must accept
them at its own risk. The only visible difference between
the two hanks is that the one which is left in is the bank
with Government influence, and the one « hiolt is left out
stands on its own bottom. Also, the Q.N., whose, notes
are made legal tender, is in much the worse odour of the
two, and its 70s. shares are vainly offered at half a-crown
without finding a buyer !
Premier Bibbs, with his usual illogical roar,
declared that he “didn’t intend to allow ihe B.N.Q. to
establish branches all over the province and issue paper,’
but yet he allows the Q.N.B. to do that same thing undr r
exactly similar circumstances—or rather, under worse
circumstances, for while the Government £oes out of its
way to cancel the B.N.Q.’s existing note-issue it deliber-
ately invites the other bank, which has at present no note-
issue at all in N.S.W., to start one forthwith and shovel
out paper. Further, it is a curious tact that the B.N.Q.
was omitted in the first place, was then included in the
Bill by the special intervention of the Government, and is
now thrown out also by special intervention of the
Government. The whole business smells badly of Brisbane
Fat Man influence, and should be inquired into on that
account.
♦ * * *
Mr. R, H. D. White’s new banking bill provides
that no single individual or partnership shall be allowed
to call itself a bank in N.S.W., and that the term shall
apply only to corporations with not less than £100,000 of
paid-up capital. As has here been said, an institution
with £100,000 paid-up isn’t necessarily any more sound
than one with £1000, hut the great merit of the measure
is that it abolishes that kind of “bank” in which one
man holds 99 per cent, of the shares, nothing paid-up,
and the other nominal members of the corporation consist
of his junior clerks and office boys. Under the new system
this individual may hold 99 per cent, of the shares if he
likes, but as this will involve the finding of £99,000 in
cash, the Thomas Smith Richardson variety of banker is
practically put out of court.

Allan & Go., the Melbourne music “warehouse-
men,” pronounced their insolvency last week. Principal
cause—the failure of H. W. Farrar & Co. and subsequent
pressure by the Commercial Bank. The story of Fayrars
business transactions is likely to prove a Bankruptcy
Court romance.
* * * *
The number of big insolvents who set forth in
their schedules that they “ are being pressed by the
Commercial Bank of Australia which is a creditor for a
large amount” is a dismal fact for the reconstituted
shareholder.
• ♦ » *
The true inwardness of reconstruction is shown
by the case of the Coonamhle Hospital, which has a very
much fixed deposit of £200 with the A.J.S. Bank. The
committee wanted to operate on this. Bank replied,
stating that, as a favour, they would be allowed to draw
£100, for which 8 per cent, interest would be charged.
As the Bank only gives 4J per cent., it actually makes 3-}
per cent, by letting the committee use its own cash!
Which, of course, is typical of thousands of cas s. Last
year’s panic legislation is nothing but a scheme, and a
successful scheme, to bleed the country for the benefit
of bank shareholders.

In Melbourne lasb week, in the case of
M'Laughlin v. Bank of Victoria, an expensive Judge and
sundry high-priced barristers decided that, when a man
holds bank-shares as executor of a deceased person’s
estate, and is registered as such, the bank can’t collar the
shares as security for the executor’s private over<Wt.
You wouldn’t think such a case needed arguing.

It was Nathaniel Cork, London manager of the
Commercial Bank of Sydney, who so cruelly gave aw; y
young Peel in the matter of the now famous “State
document,” says Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent.
The paper was shown (it is averred “quietly ”) to Mr.
Cork, who sent copies of it to Mr. T. A. Dibbs. The
Treasury people still maintain that it was in no way a
public document, and they profess to be most indignant
at its having been made public. But it is strange that
the Australian newspaper people did get hold of and use
it, and that the document bears the imprimatur of the
official publishers. A scapegoat must be found, for the
Peel influence is strong enough protect the trans-
gressor. . , ,
M'Kinnon’s demand (in N.S.W. Parliament)
lor a State audit of all banks coming under the Bank
Issue Act roused up Young, the dreary person from the
Hastings and Manning electorate, who remarked that
“the Government might as well appoint auditors to
examine all the accounts of every individual in the com-
munity.” Which would be very logical if the Govern-
ment proposed to make the I O U of every individual m
the community a legal tender, which the public must take
entirely at its own risk. As it doesn’t propose this, the
comparison is pure idiocy.
* * * *
By order of the English law courts, the direc-
tors of the reconstructed N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co are being publicly examined as to the doings of that
institution, and the results are somewhat remarkable.
The proceedings have not got very far as yet, but so far
as they have got they ate interesting. According to the
cable summary, the co. obtained a legal opinion so far
back as ’79 that the debenture-holders had no special
security over the assets, but the statement that they had
such security was left standing in the prospectus. - ’ The
balance-sheet of ’91 represented interest as being paid
which was really outstanding, and a statement re the
co.’s losses was altogether suppressed, the shareholders
being left to believe that no such losses had been made.
The reason of the suppression was that the document was
“ too strong.”
The ’92 balance-sheet is also said to have sup-
pressed a number Of things. Among the bad investments
of the co. was a loan of £40,000 to a director on the
security of certain land, which one witness described as
“a swamp.” This is as far as the evidence has got yet,
and it is a bad beginning. Yet the N.Z.L. and M.
is apparently one of the most honest of the
reconstructed institutions of Australasia. The differ-
ence is that the others got through without any
public examination of anybody. The shareholders
simply accepted the unsupported statements of the direc-
tors, notwithstanding the tact that the suspension proved
their last balance-sheet, in most cases, to be mere bun-
kum, and there was no evidence that their present
statements were not bunkum also. If a public inquiry
had been made similar to that now going on in the case
of the N.Z.L. and M. Co., half the busted banks would
never have started again.

Probably one of the prettiest water-side
townships in N.S.W. is the Toronto Estate, on
the shores of the beautiful Lake Macquarie.
.Toronto is a new summer resort among some of
the loveliest scenery in this continent, and is so
rapidly growing in favour that it promises ere
long to be the Brighton of N.S. Wales. A grand
parade has been laid out by the sea-shore ; there
are piers, hotels, splendid fishing and boating,
railway and steamboat communication, and a
poem by The Bulletin’s old friend, Nelson P.
Whitelocke, in the advertisement. The Excelsior
Land Co., 171 York-street, Sydney, is selling
allotments at Toronto at a reasonable figure.

Thomas Henry Hill, one of the famous
Six Hundred, has died at Enfield, S.A. He
arrived in S.A. in 1866 and entered the police
within a week of landing. Subsequently, of
course, he kept a public-house—in Adelaide. He
was a man of good character, and died at the age
of 63.
Louis Stevenson gets all his swear-words
into Sydney Daily Telegraph without any diffi-
culty. Once that paper published serially a novel
by Harold Stephen, Australian journalist, and
every time he wrote “ damn ” the editor commu-
nicated with him, saying “ we cannot allow you
to be coarse.”
Laurens, the just-departed Vic. M.L.A.,
was a Jerseyite, but generally passed for a Swiss,
which increased the wonder over his prodigious
industry. He was the most ingenious pronouncer
of English ever heard in Parliament, but had a
lot of now*. The big Speight v. Syme action arose
out of his borrowings.

CHARLES MONTGOMERY,
a constable in the Bridge-street burglary.)
One of the “books of the day” is “A
Yellow Aster,” by Mrs. Mannington Caffyn', a
name well known to Bulletin readers. The
novel is already in its fourth edition, and pro-
mises to be as popular as “Dodo,” or “The
Heavenly Twins.” There is nothing Australian
about it. Dr. Caffyn, by the way, is getting
together a decent practice in London.
“ The Earl of Coventry,” says the Argus’
own special Duchess, “is very proud of his son
Henry, who has done something to restore credit
to the family name.” Henry has been helping to
mow down the Matabele. The old nobility is
easily glorified. The Coventry family will pout
its chest over the fat nigger whom Henry prodded
with a bayonet for the next hundred years.
Madame Belle Cole, now on her way to
sing in Australia, is even fatter, than Antoinette
Sterling. She lives in one of those queer, quiet,
little London squares opening out of a busy
thoroughfare, and, seen in her tiny drawing-room,
there seems absolutely nothing in the place but
Belle Cole and piano, chiefly the former. Erst-
while the popular contralto greatly favoured a
song commencing “ Oh, Fatima 1 ”

The late James Swan, Q. M.L.0., and
once owner of Brisbane Courier, was an illegiti-
mate son of Private Jas. Swan, killed in the
Peninsular war. His mother was a deaf mute
named Janet M’Laren, who was murdered in his
presence by another woman named Mary Muck-
straffie, jealousy causing the quarrel. Swan was
then a boy of 13. He subsequently came to Aus-
tralia at the instance of Dr. Lang, and made a
fortune of £50,000, half of which went to his
young wife when he died intestate at 81. £25,000
is now going a-begging in Glasgow for those who
can prove relationship to Swan’s father or mother.
Here is a romance in real life which rivals most
creations of fiction.
Tom Playford, going to London as S.A.
Agent-General, will be like a chawbacon in a
lady’s boudoir. Playford and the Vic. Speaker
Bent are brothers in Christian names and brothers
in profession. Both graduated as honest dung-
tossers and market-gardeners. Playford is as
uncouth as Bent in social forbearances, but twice
as manly and not nearly so vulgar as the -First
Commoner of the Cabbage Garden.
David Symo, of the Age, receives £l2OO
“ as a genuine token of the feeling of the colony ”
upon his libel-case. In a day or two, Speight will
be presented with exactly the same amount, or a
trifle more, as a similar token of equal genuine-
ness. Only, the presentation to Speight will not
bo a subject for three solid columns of report and
rubbish in Syme’s newspaper. There is no fear of
the Age rushing into contempt of court on behalf
of Speight.
At a picnic given at Melbourne in honour
of Sara Bernhardt, a well-known owner of broad
acres and big flocks asked to be introduced to the
mighty woman of tragedy and “side.” “ What is
he?” loftily enquired the haughty goddess (who
was herself a milliner’s apprentice in the mi«ty
years aback). “A squatter,” was the reply of
the young- sculptor addressed. “ Ugh ! ” she
indignantly pouted, “ a wholesale butcher ! I do
not want to know him,” and moved away.

Madame Belloc, the writer of the article
on George Eliot in the current Contemporary, is
a first cousin of Henry Parkes, of N.S.W. Her
name is Bessie Parkes Belloc. , The “ Essays on
Women’s Work,” which she wrote as far back as
’64, was one of the earliest books published “at
’Ome ” on the woman question. She is an interest-
ing old lady with white hair, who knew intimately
the Brownings, G. H. Lewis, George Eliot, and
nearly all the well-known literary people of a
generation ago.
Lord Augustus Loftus has finished the
second portion of his “Recollections.” As a
Privy Councillor Lord Augustus is bound by oath
not to reveal State secrets, so he has submitted
proofs to Lord Salisbury and Lord Rosebery, past
and present Foreign Secretaries', who have both
highly approved of what he has written. As soon
aS -vi^j * JO °^ : * s i ssue< 3 by Cassel’s, Lord Augustus
will depart for the continent, there to end his
days. By-the-way, “Monty” Loftus is now
British Consul in Madrid, and is attached to the
embassy there.

The news of S.A. Agent-General Bray’s
mental illness hardly surprised those who have
watched the dropsical fattening of the little man
ever since his ennobblement. Bray had an active
little mind in an active little body, but he also
had a very little soul. Quirky as a solicitor, he
was insignificant as a politician. He ran with the
hare and hunted with the hounds. He pretended
advanced democracy, but his heart was always in
in nded fleshpots left him by heredity. He
talked republicanism, but snapped greedily at a
title at the second offer, excusing himself on the
miserable quibble that, as Speaker, he would have
insulted that office by declining it ! He aped to
be a chieftain in the Natives’ Association, but ran
away to live in England at the first chance.
Brown-S&juard, the Franco-American
physiologist just deceased, was known chiefly to
the world as the inventor of an Elixir of Life
widely advertised a few years ago. His elixir
which was to make the bald-head once more
eligible for the dress-circle, consisted of a prepara-
tion from the organs of the guinea-pig. This, the
basis of his popular fame, sounded like quackery ;
nevertheless, Brown-Sequard was a truly brilliant
man of science, and the associate of such famous
men as Charcot and Vulpian. He was a Creole, bear-
ing in bis beady dark eyes strong evidences of the
tar-brush,” and was a nice, shiny, comfortable,
white-bearded old gentleman who looked like Papa
forpy,. of N.S.W. Assembly, and shook hands
according to the fish-like manner of “ the greatest
of Australian statesmen.”
A Bulletin correspondent:—
Please don’t sneer, even in a roundabout way, at
1 a ikes poverty. The old man would not be so
poor had he not insisted upon paying all his creditors,
who are too poor to bear Ihe loss, 20s. in the £ despite
having obtained releases from them. Since his assign-
-88 he has paid £llOO of debts from which his
r e ™. 9* assignment legally released him. Such conduct
in Uibbs is always thought worthy of being trumpeted
about; in Parkes it gives rise to jibes at his poverty. I
happen to know that this is no new thing. Parkes has
never regarded either bankruptcy or a release as a bar to
any claim by a, creditor for a debt incurred by him on his
own account if the creditor were in want of money. The
interest on the fund subscribed for the old man has always
gone to support his children by his first wife and other
dependents—l 6 in all. Parkes himself has never touched
one penny of it. Farther, his account for carriage-hire
for the years during which he was unable to walk
amounted to several hundreds. Under these circum-
stances it is not very surprising that he should again be
nara-up, but it is surprising that his poverty should be so
differently regarded from the poverty of, say, Dibbs.
Very Rev. Hussey Burgh Macartney,
Bean of Melbourne, achieved another birthday on
Tuesday of the present week, when this striking
example of clerical longevity entered his 96th
year. Ordained a deacon of Limerick nearly 72
y™ rs ago, Hussey Macartney was already a
middle-aged man at the time of his arrival in
Melbourne with Bishop Perry—January 3rd,
He has added 46 years of “colonial experi-
ence ’ to his peaceful history, and is still preach-
Since 1851 he has held the position of Dean
of Melbourne, laying up occasionally considerable
treasures on earth, it is said, in that long mean-
time. Neither brilliant nor broad-minded, the
Dean of Melbourne is at least a competent theo-
logian of the old, Jonah-and-the-whale school;
also, the fact that his father was an Irish baronet
is an excuse for his inherent Toryism. Whatever
may be said for, or against, his Scriptural teach-
ing, it were absurd to deny the claims of Dean
Macartney—the possessor of a sound mind in a
nonogenarian body—to rank as a truly remarkab’o
person and parson.
PHOTO. BY FOSTER & MARTIN, COLLINS-ST., MELR.
THE DEAN OF MELBOURNE, Born April 10, 17[?]
How Victoria goes to bed :
Two maids undress the Queen. Before she gets into
bed, they heat and spread between the Irish linen sheets
a white blanket, part wool, part cotton, and bound with
white silk ribbon, with the royal arms embroidered in the
corner. With brandy and water for a nightcap, Queen
Victoria reclines upon the blanket until her body is
thoroughly heated. The maids then withdraw‘the
blanket, and the finest product of Ireland’s loom embraces
the Queen of England and the Empress of India.
And does she snore much ?

Mrs. Ross, High-st., Waratah, N.S.W., who
writes on January 25,1893 —About twelve months
ago I had a severe attack of influenza, which left
me in a very weak state. Although I was attended
by a doctor I derived no benefit from his medicine.
I had a complete loss of appetite attended by
continual headache and dizziness ; at times I felt
as if I could not live a day longer. I had given
up all hopes of ever recovering. On seeing Mrs.
Dent, of Lambton’s, testimonial in the newspaper,
and knowing the lady personally, I resolved to try
Clements Tonic. Before I had finished the first
bottle all the disagreeable symptoms vanished.
I then took another bottle and felt entirely cured.
I am only too happy to recommend Clements Tonic.
I believe it is the best medicine in the world for a
weak and debilitated constitution.—Yours grate-
fully, Mrs. Ross, High-street, Waratah, N.S.W.

POLITICAL POINTS
Prospective S.A. deficit on 30th June,
£60,000.
Maoriland revenue returns for March
quarter are expected to show a £200,000 surplus.
The advance towards prohibition in Maori-
iand will at least retard woman-suffrage in Aus-
tralia.
M‘llwraith objects to manhood suffrage.
He only believes in the electoral rights of “things,”
not of “men.”
A favourite expression on “King”
Stddon’s lips is: “I am not Dick Seddon now;
Lut the Premier of Maoriland.”
The Queensland Labour members now
style themselves the “Progress ” party, and their
opponents the “ Plunder ” party.
Walker, M.P., has at last got his Distress
for Rent Abolition Bill through N.S.W. Assembly.
It will, of course, be “ slung out ” of the House of
Landlords.
A year ago, there were 30 lepers at N.S.W.
leper station, at Little Bay. Now there are 36.
Whatever else may fail the good old leper turns
up regularly.
“Beauty draws us with a single hair.”
Yet Parkes illogically argues that Westraliana
favour Federatu n because they rushed to hear
him lecture.
G D. Clark, M.P., declares that the
U.S.W. Labour Electoral League wants to enforce
a new electioneering principle—the one-member-
one-term vogue.
S.A. Labour programme for L.O. elections
contains, as the main plank, the increase of the
land values tax to one penny, with a corresponding
remission of revenue duties.
What a satire ! A public meeting held to
protest against the opium traffic on the very day
that a Sydney publican is fined for selling sul-
phuric acid rot-gut as wholesome whisky.
Bacon: “Many things are assumed to
exist because they have had names given to them.”
The “ Anglo-Saxon race,” for instance. Also,
“British fair play” and Australian “loyalty !”
Adelaide believes that Westralia’s prospec-
tive advance to the dignity of a first-class province
will make more roseate the chances of the holy
city’s selection as Australia’s federal capital.
The House of Kossuth has swallowed its
vjmit. Before the old man’s ptomaines were fully
developed, his son had, “after consideration,”
decided to swear allegiance to the Hapsburgs.
The Beacon (Max Hirsch) : “What is the
Argus about? For 20 years and more it has
opposed Protection in all its forms, and now,
when victory is near it turns round and advocates
it.”
CONVENIENT.
Dr, Renwick having said it was so cold in Chicago that his jaw was frozen “like a
bar of iron,” this paper makes a little suggestion to several jawsters.
Ex-Premier M‘llwraith has expressed his
confidence in the discretion of the Queensland
Parliamentary Labour-party. The confidence of
the wolf in the lamb, of the burglar in the poisoned
house-dog.
The N.S.W. National Mule is considering
a suggestion that every elector be legally com-
pelled to vote at each Parliamentary election. The
Mule is, therefore, moving to ensure its own
speedy funeral.
Sydney has evolved a “ German Political
Union,” set upon influencing the coming N.S.W.
elections. Its platform is—Freetection, Single-
trade, Pro-tax, Imperial Federation, Republi-
canism, German Colonisation, Labour Concilia-
tion, Freedom of Contract, and laws to prohibit
over-production. Vich blatform can be aldered
to suit eferybodies.

Victorian land selectors, .£30,000 annual interest.
Do Patterson and Co, expect this to be paid,
while they are giving away land and money to
the village settlers ?
“ The figure of speech is a strong one, but
it scarcely overstates the imminence of the peril
which confronts the country, while still drifting
to leeward at the rate of £l5OO a day.”—Melb.
Age, April 3, 1894.
To keep an appointment with his con-
stituents, Labour-member M'Donald (Q.) lately
rode 125 miles in a day and a-half, qver bad roads
and flooded country. Yet people wonder that
the Labour-party prospers!
The S.A. Govt, newspaper-advertising
bills are now the smallest in Australasia. How-
ever, this is not all gain, as the papers have more
than proportionately cut down their telegraphic
messages as a quid pro quo.
The British House of Commons has passed
by ten votes a motion in favour of Home Rule for
Scotland. The two Unions, both carried through
by bribery and open, shameless corruption, are
falling to pieces almost simultaneously.
After a vigorous teetotal canvass for votes
on local option at Rockdale (N.S.W.) municipal
election, one voter only turned up. The cost of
collecting and enumerating him was five guineas,
and on scrutiny his paper was discovered to be
informal.
The democratic vote is sadly split at S.A.
Council election. For the two vacant Central
seats, two labour-men, one independent, and one
progressive candidate woo the democratic vote,
and one Ass. candidate, Mayor Willoox, gets the
block property vote.
A poor Queensland Labour-member, the
other day, renewed a .£2O bill-of-sale over his
personal effects. No man who is at the mercy of
a bill-discounter for his household goods can freely
work in Parliament for the Labour-party. Nor
can he for £l5O a year.
About a thousand Chinese, farming 5000
acres, are settled on the Herbert, N.Q. Rents
from £1 per acre upwards. Chinese sampans on
the river; Chinese costumes in the field by day ;
tom-toms and opium by night. Come now —Is
the Caucasian played out ?
An assertive M.L.A. wrote to the chair-
man of a meeting in his (N.S.W.) district the
other day: “My views are exactly the same as
when you last elected me,” and was astonished to
find that the meeting resolved : Therefore, he was
not a fit and proper person.
Minister for Lands Barlow (Q.) has evi-
dently a bad memory, otherwise he would not
have forgotten that in May, ’BB, he described his
present friend, MTlwraith, as “Barabbas.” This
little fact did a lot to put Labour-member Wil-
kinson in for Ipswich, Liars and politicians need
good memories.
Accidental admission by Sydney D.T. :
Were the people to go away the (railway) lines would
be worth what they would fetch as old iron, while the un-
alienated Crown lands would have no value at all.
Corollary: Were the people to go away, the
alienated lands of the province would also have no
value at all.
It is anomalous that under the N.S.W.
Electoral Act, even in its improved state, a man
may be more effectually disfranchised by changing
his residence, perhaps only a stone-throw, than
by committing a crime. From the date of his
arrest until he is sentenced, if he can get bail, he
can vote, but if he change his residence he, for
three months, no matter what equivalent to bail
he offers, is as completely deprived of his franchise
rights as if he had been sentenced to three months
imprisonment.
In N.S.W., 257,320 of the names on the
electoral rolls are those of men who don’t own a
square foot of land. On the other hand, 677
persons (not all voters) own 21,000,000 acres of
N.S.W. soil, or nearly half the alienated land. If
there be any virtue in manhood suffrage, the next
election should immediately precede some notable
reforms.
To show the absolute absurdity of titles,
what truly great man is ever mentioned but by his
name alone! The only exception to that rule
is Lord Edward Fitzgerald, whom the Irish still
continue to honour by his full title, because they
were surprised that there could be discovered a
lord with manhood enough to fight and die for the
rights of the people.
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have re
duced by 10 per cent, all salaries over £2OO a year,
and cut down the days worked by wages-men
from ten to nine a fortnight. This is unpleasant;
but the Commissioners say there is no other way
to make ends meet. The Bulletin trusts they
won’t need reminding to raise salaries again when
revenue improves.
Cardinal Moran wants religious schools to
abolish selfishness. His philosophy is unsound.
Self-interest is, and must remain, the mainspring
of human endeavour. It only needs enlightenment,
and this is given by scientific morality, which
very often conflicts with the current religions.
“ Every creed is a rock in running water,
Humanity sweeps by it.”
The working classes are no better and no
worse than other classes, says the condescending
Argus. But inasmuch as toil is a great discipli-
narian and humaniser, it is certain that the

working classes have a moral, as well as physical,
advantage over the class that doesn t work at all.
The least intellectual kind of labour is better for

Federation ! Do not most of the political
schemes savour of an attempt to chloroform
democracy by placing the executive far from the
direct pressure of the constituencies? Especial
danger lies in the proposal to have an Upper
House elected by the Parliaments. The manifold
evils of the present representative system would
be but the cube-root of the evils of such a plan.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary Labour-party
has unanimously decided not to sign the pledge
drawn up by the central committee of the Labour
Electoral League. The central committee, some
time ago, decided that every candidate who didn’t
sign the pledge should be opposed at the polls,
and should not be recognised as a genuine Labour
candidate, so war is apparently declared on both
sides.
Chief Justice Way, of S.A., had arranged
to visit Brother Duff in Sydney, but Premier
Kingston found that Mr. Way’s letters-patent as
Lieut.-Governor did not provide for the appoint-
ment of a deputy to act in his absence. So he had
to stay at home. Yet Mr. Way did once absent
himself from the province, and appointed Justice
Bundey to act for him. The legal nose here smells
complications.
Direct and proportional taxation of wealth
is the true policy of democracy. The single-tax
on land is not sufficient. It must be supplemented
by a tax on incomes, or a tax on property apart
from land. Mr. Asquith, British Home Secre-
tary, shelves the question of the abolition of mine
royalties, because it would cost the country
.£130,000,000. The remedy is not to abolish them,
but to tax them —burst them up as Maoriland
bursts up the big estates.
Bitter complaints in Bananaland re matters
judicial. Three Supreme Court Judges in the
South, two in the North, three District Court
Judges, with Crown Prosecutors and other officials
at their beck and call—all for a paltry popula-
tion of some 400,000. If a case is set down for
hearing at the Supreme Court in Brisbane, ten to
one you find it is the Judge’s vacation week, a,nd
the Judge on duty refuses to take an application
set down for his “ learned brother.”
In reply to a question, the other day, as to
whether a N.S.W. Judge had recently assigned
his estate, Premier Dibbs replied : “ I think I
know every member of the Supreme Court Bench
at the present moment, and I do not think that
any one of the Judges would fill the high and
responsible office which he holds in a manner
derogatory to the position.” Dibbs is not usually
a success at giving evasive answers, but the above
pompous and dreary platitude isn’t bad.
The A.N.A. (Vic.) introduces its rules
with a statement that it takes no part in party
politics. On the strength of this it has gained
many benefit-members. But at its Warrnambool
conference, the other day, it took a stand on the
One-man-one-vote and Woman’s Suffrage ques-
tion. However desirable these may be in them-
selves, does this action not savour of breach of
faith, perhaps even breach of trust ? Does not its
action in this respect bring it under the unrepealed
conspiracy laws of George 111. ?
A Cape of Good Hope journal remarks, in
a purely business tone, that the flogging of natives
for walking on the pavements at Johannesburg
“ is having a bad effect on the labour-market.”
The fact that it may be having a bad effect on the
hinder part of the native doesn’t seem to enter
into the question at all.
“ Every institution holds within itself the
seeds of its own destruction.” This dragon-tooth
seed of society is anarchism. Society is centri-
petalism, anarchy centrifugalism. Society and
anarchism are the action and reaction of the
herding tendency of men. Bakunin is the Huss
of Society, the Wickliffe of civilisation. And as
the Lollards were crushed into contempt by
mediaeval Catholicism only to burst forth in Re-
formation flames, so anarchism will become a
byword first, and then a fire of purification.
Henri, the boy-Anarchist,
now under death-sentence at
Paris: “When the working
classes succeed, they are ab-
sorbed into the infernal bour-
geoisie. It is like a cask that has
once contained vinegar. You
may put the finest wine into it
ever afterwards, but nothing but
vinegar will come out.”
Premier Patterson rose
the other day over the festive
board, amid the grease and the
crumbs, and with rich wine drib-
bling down his beard, and ex-
plained that the depression
wasn’t such a bad thing after
all, because it had “ brought
the churches more in touch with
the masses,” and “caused a
more general and true Christian
feeling than at any former
period,” The Bulletin wants to
ask this heartless ex-slaughter-
man one question. Supposing
he had lost his little all in some
bank run by a number of fraudu-
lent church members, and was
in the habit of sleeping in a
dust-bin, and looking for aged
crusts in ash-boxes, and was an
object of scorn to all beholders,
how much better would he feel
for the knowledge that the
church was “ more in touch with
the masses”? The rich man
who throws hunks of stale gib-
berish like this to the hungry
and wretched is a hog by in-
stinct and a monkey by educat : on.
Tiia uncouth Governor M'Gregor, of New
Guinea, has a peculiar method of pacifying the
rampant native. First of all he thoroughly licks
the recalcitrant tribesmen, and then he makes six or
eight of the biggest of them into policemen. After
that the tribe is strictly loyal because every man
hopes some day to be made a policeman also. The
yearning of the Papuan to be a “trap” is some-
thing incredible. But it is absolutely necessary
to lick the tribesmen first of all, else they would
rise in insurrection and insist on being all made
policeman.
Sherbrooke’s “humorous illustration of
democracy” is gleefully quoted by Melb. Argus
reduced:—
Democracy is like a crowded omnibus wherein the
passengers try to turn the driver off the box and take the
reins themselves.
Conservatism is also like a crowded omnibus on a
bad road, wherein the passengers are all asleep,
having cheerfully trusted themselves to a driver
who may be blind and paralytic, but who is bus-
driving because he is a deceased ’bus-driver’s
eldest son.
Re the perennial topic of establishing a
morning Protectionist paper in Sydney—“ What’s
the good?” lately asked Dibbs. “It’s the
hostility of the Freetrade press that has kept me
in office. Take Tom Slattery, where would he be
if the Freetrade press had power ? Where would
Copeland be? Not a cent of mine will ever go
into a Protectionist paper; better, like See, buy
shares in a Freetrade paper. Far better for our
crowd to be denounced by the Herald and Tele-
graph than to be praised by an organ of our
own.”
Dear Bulletin, —Isn’t it rather premature
to talk of bringing the deluded and disbanded;
Rue-Australians back? The Royal Tar will soon
be back to take another lot, who are ready and
waiting to go. And why not ? You have given
them to understand that if things do go wrong
the good old Government won’t see them stranded.
Thus, you deliberately encourage new recruits to
make fools of themselves by taking away the
natural penalty of their folly. In fact, now I
know Dibbs will bring me back if I don’t like it, I
have serious thoughts of going to Rue' Australia
myself. lam fond of travel, and a sea voyage
will do me all the good in the world.—Yours, A.


Moreover, Mr.' Patterson has got
hold'of the great truth that if we want
to sell to other countries we must
be willing to buy from them. And
this, of course, is the very essence of
the tariff reform argument. We receive
payment for all our products in com-
modities. Nations never send us goldj
but always goods. /I -Jj)~y Z/L $ "

-Mr. Neild asked the Premier in the
Assembly yesterday whether he was aware
that 199 half-chests of rubbish, dangerous to
tile public health, had been sold at auction
the previous day under the guise of “ tea ”
at 3jd per lb. He would hand the lion,
gentleman a sample of it, which he was told
was colored with chemical matter.

The mam objection N.S.W. Instruction
Department has to married women being re-
tained in the service is to be found in the fact
that Australian fifth-class school-girls are noto-
rious y precocious. Besides which, giving black-
oard lessons entails a deal of tiresome arm-
reaching on the part of the ladies.
* • »
When the officers of the Baratarian Navi'
gation Co. are in various ports, and while the
boats are empty of passengers, it is their custom
to hold conviviaHittle suppers, to which guests ot
both sexes are invited. This fact happened to
come to the ears of the various W.C.T.U.s of the
ports en route, who lately were impudent enough
to petition the company for an immediate sup-
pression of this “vice.” The co. curtly replied
that it had every confidence in its officers, and
was perfectly satisfied with the manner in which
their duties were performed.

The W.C.T.U. has appointed a committee
to inquire into the condition of Australian bar-
maids. Lough! Let the barmaids respond with
t(> ' nc J u ' re into the condition of the
W.b. I. U.
• • tt
“A Barmaid,” in Sydney Telegraph:
I have never been compelled to witness sights or
listen to any language that would shock even the
sisters of the W.C.T.U.” “Even” is good.
When asked to suggest employment for bar-
maids deprived, by legislation, of their present
means of living, the W.C.T.U. shrieks : “ Let
them can fruit! ” In a word, let them compete
on equal or less than equal terms with a class of
female labour which now gets but 10s. or 15s.
weekly with food thrown in. The average bar-
maid is a good-looking woman, and her beauty is
no doubt part of her stock-in-trade just as the
beauty of many a good-looking and virtuous
actress, or tradesman’s wife who serves in her
husband s shop, is part of her stock-in-trade.
Sour hagdom naturally revolts from the idea that
a woman’s beauty should he of any pecuniary or
other advantage to her. But it is, and it is going
to be for ever and ever ; and the last pretty bar-
maid, The Bulletin hopes, will only disappear
when, by Act of the Federal Legislature, the
vineyards of Australia are uprooted because a
number of impotent males and anaemic virgins in
CANDOUR.
BENEVOLENT MAN ( condoling with Mrs. O'Hooligan, who has a large family) :
And yet, withal, my good woman, you would be very sorry to
lose one of them, wouldn’t ye now?”
Mrs. O’HOOLIGAN; : “Well, yis, but ye see, sorr, they costs more to bury
nor to kape.”
Parliament assembled have ukased that there is
to be no more wine.
Let The Bulletin say it again. The Aus-
tralian “ teetotal question ” all resolves itself into
this : “ Are you prepared to uproot the vine-
yards?” If you are, then you are the sort of
crank who would shut down the gold-mines
because they give rise to gambling.
* * *
The International Christian Police Asso-
cintion is one of the fluffy refulgences of fin-de-
sitcle, female-pushed reform. A Miss Mary E.
Raymond has established a Sydney branch of the
same and has invited the armed guardians of the
peace to attend a social gathering and become
Christianised. The potentialities of the situation
slip from imagination’s grasp. The “copinaji ”
spirituosised we know, but the “copman ” spirit-
ualised is a being beyond the reach of fancy. The
fad is simply a fascinating phase of feminine
lunacy, and therein lies the secret of its origin.
The female fancy, in countries where real, blood-
dabbling don’t perennially rage, feeds
principally on policemen. The Bulletin prefers
its policemen in the heathen state, and would
rather trust them with revolvers than with
gimlets.

Roberts, the new Victorian flagellator, is
a complete success after all. His first attempt
was so poor that the “authorities” thought of
requesting him to send in his resignation, but last
week a burglar had to be operated upon, and to
the satisfaction of all right-thinking persons
Roberts “wielded the lash very severely.”—
In Melbourne,
last Thursday, a
young man was sent
to gaol for twelve
months for attempt-
ing to pick a lady’s
pocket. It after-
wards transpired
that the lady had
no pocket, and as
nobody can attempt
to steal from a place
when there is no
such place to steal
from, any reason-
ably smart lawyer
could presumably
have got him off on
a beautiful point of
law. But he hadn’t
the means to pay a
lawyer, so the sta-
tutes fell upon and
flattened him.
