A man who, five years ago, was about the
smartest and best-known financier in Australia,
can now be met with almost any day in a shirtless
•and almost shoeless condition, his principal gar-
ments being a tattered pair of pants and a singlet.
He used to have the most gorgeous suite of offices in
the city, and his mansion was a palace on a small
scale. He was a long, dark, silent, unhappv-
looking, keen-eyed man in those days, and he looks
just about the same now, barring the tatters and
the absence of the shirt. Since his fall he has been
so absolutely forgotten that hardly anyone seems
to be aware of his existence, and his unshaven
spectre wanders about and looks blankly at the
old suite of offices, where another man runs the
old business, and passes almost without recogni-
tion. The other day, however, a former clerk
came upon the greasy apparition of his ex-
employer leaning against a lamp-post. He
was gaunt and wild-looking, and he wore
the unkempt aspect of a man who cuts his
own hair with a nail in the Domain. “ I was
just wondering where in all the world yon had got
to,” said the clerk; “there’s a man needs your
signature to fix up some old trust business. He’ll
give yon two guineas tor it,” “ Two guineas !”
yelled the man of lost tens of thousands, as he
jumped in the air with excitement and grabbed at
the atmosphere with ten horny fingers ; “ where
is he ?”
Almost alongside this fallen magnate’s mansion
THE BRIGHT LEXICON OF BUST.
HEAVY MELB. FATHER: “With energy and perseverance every young
man can succeed. There’s no such word as fail! ”
HOPEFUL SON: “You’re right there, Father,— it’s reconstruct.”
is what was once the gorgeous residence of another
financier. This financier is also fallen—in fact, he
is in gaol. There is material here for a picture to
be called “The Prodigal’s Return.” It should
represent the present owner of one of these
luxurious establishments smoking a big, fat
cigar on the tiled verandah on a moonlight
night, and, just visible against the full moon, the
broken hat and tattered, spiky hair of the former
owner who has stopped in his drift through life
to look over the fence at the scene of his former
greatness. There are a good many such men around
Sydney, and they make instinctive pilgrimages to
their old homes, and heave sighs over the garden
railings. Therefore, when a man has risen to
sudden wealth and has taken a big and garish-
looking house, the apparition of the former owner
groaning outside the gate serves as a solemn
lesson upon the uncertainty of riches. But the
man inside seldom heeds the lesson, and generally
sools his dog at the apparition.
* * *
The hitherfco-profitable game of joining
the Young Men’s Christian Association is quite
played out. The godly youth, who formerly made
the Association a stepping-stone to a position of
trust, has so frequently been bowled out, and
bowled into gaol, that no really level-beaded
merchant will take a Y.M.C.A. man on at any
price. By the way, the Melb. branch has had to
abandon its pretentious new building in Bourke-
street, through inability to pay interest on the
mortgage.
* * #
A certain smart man bas somehow got
himself appointed receiver of English remittances
for a wild young Irishman. The Irish youth
i nagines that the remittances reach his friend at
the end of each month, whereas they really arrive
in the middle. Just about the time the trustee gets
the com the Irishman is stone-broke, and appeals
to his good friend for advances. The “advance ”
consists of half the money the trustee has already
received, and the Milesian gives a bill for the
whole amount. Thus the noble trustee shares
.£lOO a month, has no risk, and has got in hand
bills on demand, I O IPs, and other securities
(which should have been returned to the “mug”)
amounting to some =£4ooo. The Hebrew financier
is simply a philanthropist compared to this smart
man.

The Bulletin is assured that the state-
ments made in a recent is ue by “an old member
of the Australian Club” (Melb.) were incorrect.
While the entrance tee has been temporarily re-
duced, and has now reverted to .£5O, the annual sub-
scription bas never been varied. “In common with
other first-class clubs members have the privilege
of introducing friends to dinner under certain
restrictions, but strangers are not otherwise
privileged, while the statement respecting notices
in rooms concerning thefts has absolutely no
foundation on fact.” The Australian is now said
to be in a very sound financial position.
* * *
During a prosy speaker’s address at Hobart
Church Congress, a parson’s wife, who felt drowsi-
ness fast overcoming her, borrowed a bottle of
volatile essence from a neighbour, which she in-
haled with gratifying results. Then, turning to a
gentleman next her, who appeared to be equally
somnolent, she said : “ Would you like this to
smell?” He replied : “No, thank you, madam, I
prefer to sleep.”

Be the Sydney publican found guilty of
adding sulphuric acid to his draught whisky, and
fined <£2o in the Central Police Court last week.
Had a man been caught selling good whisky
without a license the fine would have been £5O ;
and had he been caught making good whisky on
the sly and selling it he would have been fined
£lOO or sent to gaol for 12 months. A publican’s
license ooveretb a multitude of sins.

A Melb. lady tells a
sensational story about
how she was walking
along St. Kilda-road,
about 9.15, on a recent
evening, when she was
rushed by “a man 34
years of age,” who threw
her down, presented a
revolver at her head, &c.
The extreme circumstan-
tiality of this lady is her
most remarkable feature.
People are apt to be
sceptical about any female
who is assaulted at a
quarter-past nine by a
man 34 years of age.
They feel much the same
about her as they do about
the person who is stunned
at midnight by being
struck on the head from
behind by a dark man,
sft. llin. high, with one
back tooth missing, and
who only comes to him-
self after lying insensible
for 49J minutes. The
complainant who is too
definite is lost.

In future the enfant
terrible present at
“knioker” weddings (the

This is the “ad.” which The Bulletin
has long been waiting to see, bub, so far, without
any success :
T John Smith, hereby give notice that 1 will not be
; > responsible for my own debts after this date.
(Signed) JOHN SMITH.
The BuLLETiN.submits that the above is the sort
of tiling which would supply a long-felt want.
The world is full of men who want to pay their
debts, but are constitutionally incapable of ever
having any money. Therefore, it should be com-
petent for every man of this sorb to have himself
registered as a financial lunatic, and to announce
in the papers thereafter, say every three months,
so as to keep the matter before the public, that
he isn’t responsible for his own debts. After
that, anyone who gives him credit does it en-
tirely at his own risk, for all debts due by him
are irrecoverable. If a man developes financial
lunacy and refuses to register himself, then his
friends or creditors should be empowered to have
him declared—upon the examination of two un-
biassed money-lenders, who will give the needful
certificates—-a dangerous monetary idiot, after
which will be duly advertised at his own ex-
pense. The man who isn’t responsible for his
wife’s debts doesn’t matter; very few men’s wives
get much chance to run into debt, and the notice
is generally inserted out of pure cussedness. It is
the man who isn’t responsible for his own debts
who is the cause of all the trouble.

Haunted by a phantom fear,

Church-hells in the distance pealing

When the cat begins to swish
One 1 two 1 three I begins to swish.
Roberts, starting rather badly,
Caused a lot of discontent;
Fat Man dropped his Argus sadly,

“ More than this their backs are needi.ig

stands, on “the highest authority,” that to form a
camp of instruction for the military forces of the
province would cost .£33,000. Quite so. Each
little corps must have its officers’ mess, and its
sergeants’ mess, and a fully equipped office for its
adjutant. Each staff-officer, too, must have his
office with table, pigeonholes, easy chair and other
paraphernalia. Compare with this Wellington
studying his maps under the lee of a dripping
hedge, or Frederick of Prussia sitting over the fire
in a charcoal-burner’s hut. What is asked for is
not a camp of instruction under conditions as
nearly as possible like those of actual war, but a
luxurious picnic. What is really wanted is a
week or two of marching and manoeuvring as if in
the presence of an enemy with baggage reduced to
a minimum, with officers sharing all the labours
of their men, and every duty carried out strictly
as if in the field.
m * •
A lady writes to The Bulletin :
You were, perhaps, justified in making fun of the
kniokerbooker costumes worn by the female participants
in the recent Maoriland wedding. It does not seem an
eminently suitable occasion for donning them, except as
an advertisement for the dress reform. But I hope that
your id< as on the subject of female dress are progressive.
Surely you would not desire women to be sacrificed
always, as they are now, on the altar of prejudice in this
matter. It would be such a benefit to the human race in
general, and to the feminine portion of it in particular,
if there were more freedom allowed in raiment.
Opponents of dress reform seem to imagine that the
advanced view is to give up skirts altogether ; or
else to force unhappy man into some form of
them, while woman prances around in trousers.
Why should women (or men either, for that matter) be
restricted to a single form of dress ? At present, the only
variety afforded in women’s dress is in the fashion of wide
or narrow sleeves, full or narrow skirts, plain or trimmed
bodices, jackets, polonaises, paniers, &c., recurring
periodically at the mandate of some distant arbiter. In-
stead of this, we should be able to adopt different modes
or forms of dress for different times, seasons, and pur-
poses. Why not use kniekerbocker suits for walking,
riding, boating, &c. ; short skirts of plain design for
housework ; Png dresses for indoor functions or dancing?
“ There are times,” as Mr. Grundy would say, when a
loose variety of flowing gown, or even the classic sheet
would be convenient. Shakespeare has shown
us that a Rosalind can be just as charming
and feminine in male as in female attire ; and
Portia acts up to her robes in the “Merchant of
Venice.” Of course, like a good many olher reforms, not
being fundamental, it must wait until radical reforms are
carried out. With social prejudices, caste, and poverty
in our midst, we must continue to endure the tyranny of
custom, in spite of the fact that fe male health is under-
mined to a lamentable degree for want of liberty in this
respect. So, in spite of the seeming incongruity of the
appearance of a bride in knickerbockers, still these people
have my heartiest thanks for their courage in braving
public prejudice. Original ryiirits expect to incur some
odium, hut many timid souls are thankful for them to
lead the wav ; and among these latter may be included—
Yours &c,, Rationale. 2nd April, 1894.
The widow of hangman Jones (who lately suicided
rather than hang a woman) was charged at a Melb. police-
court last week—drunk and riotous. The poor creature
has been trying to drown her sorrow, probably.
The wife of a well-known Southern mer-
chant lately departed from the family shrine with
a young relative of her husband, to whom the
latter had kindly given the shelter of his home.
The youthful-looking and flighty matron has
children almost as old as the Lothario, who, it is
said, in a thoughtless moment made the lady the
keeper of his finances, as he did subsequently of
his affections. The husband is, as yet, undecided
about divorce. He fears that freedom might
again tempt him into matrimony.
• * m
In Pentridge prison the other day, a con-
vict who had been severely flogged, without
uttering a groan, was let down and offered the
usual drink. He shook his head, and, pointing to
the man-flayer, said—“ I don’t want it; give it to
the fellow who’s been doing all the hard work.”

N.S.W. Department of Public Instruction
just now wears one vast, substantial smile over
some correspondence between a country teacher
and his ex-landlady, which has accidentally fallen
into the hands of the authorities. But the more
highly-flavoured of the billets-doux have been care-
fully sealed pending the appointment of an
Under-Secretary.
* * #
Mary Gaunt, who reports diseases and
lingering deaths for the ARGUS, has an idea:
“Instead of a Foundling Hospital with an ever-
open basket hanging on the gateway and no ques-
tions asked, would it not be better to enlarge the
Infant Asylum, to have an ever-open door?
Night and day there should be a responsible
officer in attendance, and instead of asking no
questions let him ask every particular, and let all
those particulars be kept secret as the grave.”
How like a woman ! She doesn’t like the idea of
missing all the spicy particulars. They would be
kept “ secret as the grave ” —by a select ladies’
committee, no doubt.
* * *
The accountant in a certain Sydney news-
paper office is a most thrifty man, and absolutely
devoted to what he considers the best interests of
his employers. T’other day one of the staff found
himself “docked.” “What for?” queried he.
“Good Friday,” murmured the man of books,
“You weren’t here Good Friday.” “Well,” said
the literary gent., “and this is a Christian
country !”

Two young women are being sent by the
Dundee Courier (Scotland) to gain information
as to the conditions under which women work in
Australia, Picture of a Melb, workroom, sent by
a Bulletin correspondent :
In one room there are some 20 girls who work from
9 a.m. until 6 p.m. with half-an-hour off for lunch. Some-
times they are kept until 10 or 11 at night, and do not get
any tea-money or over-time. They are paid so much a
week—it isn’t piece-work—and their wages range from
one who gets a pound to others at 155., 125., down to ss.
per week. Imagine a girl over twenty working like a
nigger all the week for the paltry sum of 55., and being
supposed to live on it— to pay for lodgings and buy her-
self 21 meals with 60 coppers 1 They don’t ask them to
take ss. per week. Oh, no ! When the miserable starved
wretches swarm in answer to an advt. for “improvers.”
expecting to get 10s. or 15s. per week, they are told:
"Oh! a little girl or two will be able to do all that we
require, and we could not give more than ss.and the
poor, hungry, half-fainting women take ss. per week and
starve on it or Some time ago the wife of a suburban
bank-manager was working in the room for ss. per week,
but her avowed object was to gain sufficient experience to
he able to make her own dresses.

How astonishingly accurate the knowledge
of Australia displayed by that gifted writer, Clark
Russell: —
“They’re long in catching Murray,” said 1. “It’s no
fault of the police. We’re not in England here. A brisk
walk takes a man into desolation. When you talk of
catching a murderer, you think of beadles and fire
engines and the electric telegraph. But the black man
is still in this country: there’s never a village pump
betwixt Woolloomooloo and the Antarctic circle. Small
wonder your bushranger flourishes."

A Melbourne Hebrew was charged, the
other day, with casually stabbing the leg of a
R. Catholic priest, whilst riding on a tram
dummy. It was represented that the accused,
who had a friend on the other side of him, sat
next to the cleric, and stuck something sharp into
his knee. Whereupon the priest asked the strange
Hebrew to apologise for making him bleed, and
the apology was duly made. Also, the priest
didn’t wish to prosecute because the alleged out-
rage occurred on Good Friday, yet the case came
into court. Having come into court it was “ dis-
missed without any evidence being called for the
defence.” Is anybody good at guessing riddles ?

Rockhampton is the headquarters of M‘ll-
- in Centralia. It is also the head-
quarters of Centralian Celestials, who have a block
of buildings almost to themselves. It also turns
out more lepers, and leper suspects, than any other
town in the North. In which matter, however,
Rockhampton takes a poetic revenge on the
Separation-hating South, for she sends all her
lepers to the southern lazaretto.

inevitably fetches up the civil servant. Sometimes

Here you see the man of £H a week —good, steady
20 and 30-year £8 a week, or more —trembling,

worm on a hook. Moral : Retrench not only with
the pruning-knife, but the axe.
# • #
In Belgium the marriage-certificate is
issued in the form of a tiny, neatly-bound book
with gilt edges. It contains, in addition to the
certificate, a summary of the marriage laws,
spaces for entering particulars regarding the birth
of 12 children, and directions for the care and
feeding of infants. There may not be much
sentiment about this, but it is practical, anyhow.
m # *
Dear Bulletin, — An interesting drunk
appeared before Melbourne City Court lately.
His speciality was a silver tube penetrating
his brisket, through which he breathed. By
reason of this mechanical contrivance, his lawyer
claimed that it was physically impossible for the
client to get drunk, as the whisky-fumes escaped
through his blow-off pipe, and never reached the
brain. If there is anything in this lawyer’s plea
for tracheotomy, you might get the medico-
plumbers to tackle “ Dipso.” An escape-valve
would possibly act where Tyson failed miserably.
—Silas Snell.

Shrimsld, of Maoriland Upper House,
declared lately, at Oamaru, that the baths were a
financial failure “because there were too many
Scotchmen in the town, and it was the Scotch-
man’s national failing not to wash.” But just
then a huge cateran, with reddish hair and two
vast freckled fists, arose, and the little Hebraic
gent, apologised hurriedly, and remarked that the
Scotch were a race with whom cleanliness was
almost a disease.

O, for the touch of the hangman’s paw,
And the sound of his vanished cough—
For a grip of the arm that served the Law
In so many a “ turning off ” !
O, for the wink of his optic bleared,
The gleam of his blood-red tbngue,
When he landed home, in a sham white beard,
With the comforting words—“ He’s ’ung” 1
A woman in widowhood’s costume groans
As she thinks of a land remote,
Where murderers hover around her Jones
And grin at his gaping throat.
Cold are the fingers that fix’t the knot
While Deeming stood on the trap.
A. woman sits drinking her grog, but not—
Ah ! not on the hangman’s lap.

sidered. For greasy, bidding was up to late rates. It
any alteration in scoured, it was slightly in buyers’
favour, consequently several lots were passed in.
Queensland clips are now arriving in Sydney, and all
consigned to our care meet with prompt attention and
are opened up and placed on the market to the best pos-

sth April, 1894.
With the Condemned
Victor Hugo, the genius to whom the world is
grateful for the marvellous conception of Jean
Valjean, makes one of his most thrilling and
analytical sketches on the thoughts of a con-
demned criminal. It is a wonderful thing—you
could believe after reading it that the author had
been himself the central figure of a condemned
cell. The agony of waiting—the frightful suspense
—the daily death the prisoner dies in his fear of
the one material death that is to come —all are
delineated with a terrible mad coolness. And,
after all, the death is really not what the man
fears, it is the certainty of it, the setting apart of
a day when he will cease to be, the thought that
after next Tuesday or Wednesday he will be in-
capable of thought—these are the terrors. It is
not the consummation but the preparation where-
in lies the punishment. We are all under sentence
of death, only we are respited indefinitely and we
do not know the date fixed for our execution.
Leaving aside accident and old age the extension
of the respite is in our own hands. With ordinary
care, rational living, and immediate attention to
the liver or kidney trouble or the broken nervous
system which seems so trifling at the outset, the
conservation of life to the limits of old age is
within the reach of everyone. Warner’s Safe
Remedies are of all medicines the key to longevity.
Their constituents are such that they assimilate
directly with the enfeebled system, and lead it
back from the grave to the sunlight. Mr. J. G.
Doxey, Produce Merchant, of Seymour, whose
letter we publish below, gives the experiences of
his truly serious condition and happy recovery :
“For thirty years I suffered from liver, kidney
and gravel diseases, and it would be impossible to
give even a faint idea of the misery and pain I
endured during that time. I was treated by
several of the leading doctors in Melbourne, but
not one of them could give me any hope of a
recovery. They expected my complaints to develop
into Bright’s disease. My back and loins always
seemed cold. After taking a few bottles of Safe
Cum I passed a quantity of clotted blood, after
which I had ease. I was so irritable that I could
not bear to have any one come near me or speak
to me. I continued taking the Safe Cure for
twelve months;! then felt I was quite cured.
My wife was afraid that the disease would return
and advised me to continue the medicine in half
doses for a further period, which I did. That
was about three years ago, and since then I have
not felt anything of the old trouble and now am
able to eat, drink, sleep and work with pleasure.
My friends never expected to see me alive to-day,
much less in the full enjoyment of all my bodily
powers. lam well-known in this district, having
resided here for about 25 years, and hundreds can
testify to the truth of these statements. I now
weigh a stone and a half more than before taking
the medicine.”

THE
'Ernest Giles Gold Prospecting
and Exploration Co,
(To be registered under the Limited Liability
Company's Act.)
CAPITAL - £6OOO in 300 Shares of £2O each,
OB' WHICH

PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS :
A. S. LOW, Esq., Merrylands, near Sydney.
CAPTAIN JOHN BROOMFIELD, Sydney.

SOLICITORS :

FELIX RANDLE, Union Bank Chambers,

THIS Company is being formed to raise a sum
of money to equip and cover the expenses
of an Exploration Party, under the command of
Mr. Ernest Giles, the well-known Australian Ex-
plorer (Gold Medallist and Fellow of the Royal
Geographical Society of London, &c.), who has
c nsented to lead the Expedition to certain gold-
bearing tracts of country in Western Australia,
already traversed by him on a previous occasion,
and he feels assured that rich auriferous deposits
abound in the country he proposes to explore.
The objects of the Company are to Prospect the
country within the Auriferous Belt, and peg out,
or otherwise acquire, all favourable discoveries on
behalf of the Company. The ground taken up
would be thoroughly tested, with the idea of
eit ier being worked by the Company, or placing
the same on the Colonial or London Markets in
separate properties.
It is estimated by Mr. Giles that it will require
a sum of about .£3OOO to properly equip the expe-
dition and cover all expenses for about 12 months,
and it is anticipated that considerably before the
expiration of that period some rich discoveries
will be made, when the balance of the subscribed
Capital could, at the discretion of the Directors,
be utilised in developing the same.
In the above estimate it must be borne in mind
that the baggage and riding camels (which will
cost some .£BOO or .£900) will be an asset of the
Company and will realise about the same amount
as they cost when finished with.
Early application is requested as over 100 shares
are already applied for.
As the whole of the money subscribed is neces-
sary for the success of the scheme, Mr. Giles has
agreed to accept the sum of £3 per week as a
nominal salary for his services, and the Company
will allot him 100 paid up Shares (which will be
divided by him amongst Members of the Party).
Each share held by a Member of the Company
will, on the payment of the above mentioned .£2O,
be deemed to be fully paid up, and shall be
without any further liability whatever.
An agreement will be drawn up by the Solicitors
between Mr. Giles and the Company, stating that
all discoveries made or Gold or other leases taken
up or acquired by any of the Exploration Party
will be the absolute property of the Company.
As it is Mr, Giles’ intention to proceed to
Western Australia immediately the Company is
formed, persons desirous of subscribing are re-
quested to do so with as little delay as possible.
Applications for shares will be received by the
Brokers, and Mr. Giles can be interviewed at
Mr. A. H. Barclay’s Offices, 19 Post Office
Chambers.
Shares will be allotted in priority of application,
and the Company will be declared formed when
150 shares are applied for.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS x*r ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpenoe, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at
ANTHONY H OBOE UN’S ?
SO THAT YOU MAY REALISE WHAT DOMESTIC ECONOMY IS!

same goods at the same prices as if you lived in town,

» * » ♦
Joseph Schellenberg, charged with selling llakd
Oasn, has been sentenced to 10 months gaol. This Hard
Cash libel business travels on an ascending scale. Bear,
who was tried first, got seven days. Then Maonamara
and Rosa got three and six months respectively, because
Bear’s sentence hadn’t proved a “deterrent.” Now
Schellenberg gets 10 months, presumably because Rosa’s
sentence wasn’t a deterrent, and the next man, if there is
a next man, to preserve the symmetry of things, will
probably get three years and a flogging. There is one
especially lamentable aspect of the ease: the Hard Cash
people might easily have said ninety-nine hundredths of
what they were indicted for without getting into gaol at
all.

“Chaffey Bros,, Limited,” are issuing 5 per
cent, first mortgage debentures up to £lOO,OOO, at a
minimum of 97 ; also, 6 per cent, cumulative preference
shares of £lO each up to £150,000. Apropos , the M‘ll-
wraith and Morehead Governments in the late eighties
refused to let the Chaffeys get an irrigation hold in
Queensland, remarking that what was a fat thing for out-
siders ought to be ditto for—“the Government.” By
which phrase was possibly meant the friends of the
Government, since they made no Government move in
the direction outlined by the Chaffey representative.

So severely have the big storekeepers in the
Vic. wheat districts been hit by the low price of wheat
that most of them decline to give any credit whatever to
farmers. Result—hundreds of men who formerly lived
on the storekeepers’ credit from seed-sowing to harvest,
are abandoning their farms.

nothing. There is enough dirt to feed a thousand stamps
for a hundred years.
* * * *
Wyalong threatens to become a plague-spot
such as Broken Hill was in the early days. When the
Barrier township was in its primitive state, waterless,
undrained, with no sanitary arrangements, and much
strange and dreadful whisky, it was no uncommon thing
for nine out of a dozen reckless and tolerably hard-
drinking young men to die inside of a very few months.
One party of eight, which started from Sydney, buried
seven in half-a-year, through typhoid and strange liquor,
and then the lone survivor borrowed the money to pay
his passage back, and took to the law. Wyalong, by all
reports, is going much the same way. Several thousand
men are camped close to a tank which supplies all the
drinking water of the community. Most of the refuse
soaks into the tank, and when it rains the putrid accumu-
lations get there all the sooner. Now that typhoid has
started there are all the materials for a really high-class
lazar-house at Wyalong.
* * * *
At Wyalong 15 barbers, 12 butchers, 7 bakers,
9 bootmakers and 10 storekeepers are sharing what money
is spent (i.e., brought) there.
* * * *
If they have a three weeks’ drought in Wyalong,
water will have to be carted two miles; a six
weeks’ drought, four miles. Whereas the first heavy rain
that comes will filter through the refuse of the camps into
the dam at present in use.
• * * »
The first out of three teams that brought the
battery shortly to be erected at Wyalong had nine black
poley bullocks out of a team of 12. Whether it is an omen
or not has yet to be discovered, but as it passed down the
main street it looked very much like a funeral-procession.

A great crush, says a Bulletin correspondent,
is always to be seen at Wyalong Post-office window on
delivery of the mail, and it is noteworthy that two-thirds
of those who receive letters have to sign for them as con-
taining money—to take them away. There is a small
fortune ahead for a pawnbroker. Horses are being
hawked for 305., a saddle and bridle went for 10s., and a
Winchester rifle for 12s. 6d.
* * * *
N.S.W. Govt, geologist has gone to Wyalong,
and may be able to decide the lay of the country. At pre-
sent, the scene is one of miscellaneous surface-scratching.
A well-defined reef, traceable on the surface for two miles,
and carrying decent gold, was found, however, last week,
six miles from the main camp; and how few men were
really making anything was shown by the fact that ths
population cleared out in a body to the new rush, the
main diggings being reported “ almost deserted.”

police. The owner has now gone up to “ make arrange-
ments ’’—for royalties, presumably. And N.S.W. Upper
House doesn’t think much of the Mining on Private Pro-
perly Bill.

In the recent London action, Aarons v. Trower,
arising out of transactions in Wentworth (N.S W.) gold
shares, defendant pleaded that Aarons promised to wipe
out his private account in consideration of being intro-
duced to two jobbers who would buy 15,000 Wentworth
shares. The court disbelieved the plea, and Aarons
walked off with a verdict for £2859 Bs. 4d. and costs.

manganese mine.

Tobacco sent from German New Guinea to
Europe realised the highest price ever obtained.
And Australian tobacco would star abroad also,
did growers only take pains.
Hard times tell on the workman’s small
luxuries, and hundreds of smokers now make their
own tobacco, buying the cured leaf and rolling and
squeezing it for themselves. The big manu-
facturers, who pay a heavy State license fee,
naturally object; but it’s not easy to see how a
man can be punished for making tobacco merely
for his own consumption, especially when he uses
Australian leaf and helps to keep another fellow
busy growing it. If he sells, Government can
fairly come down on him for revenue ; but as long
as he paddles his own canoe exclusively the
practice seems rather commendable than other-
wise.
THEY SPORT WITH HUMAN LIFE.
I’ll give you the plain facts in the case, and you
shall help me to judge whether there was a mistake
made or not.
It seems that some time in 1889—in the winter,
no doubt —Mr. Strong, of Leicester, was taken
down with influenza, often called the grip ; and
truly a strong grip it has when it once takes a
hold. Well, lam glad to say he was able to fight
out the battle and get the best of it. But the best
wasn’t anything to brag of. The influenza hadn’t
exactly beaten Mr. Strong, but left him in a very
bad condition. And here is where yon and I
properly pick up the thread of the story.
His meals didn’t tempt him ; the most savoury
of dishes had no charms for our friend. He had
lost his appetite, and in the long run a man had
better lose his money. He forced down something,
of course, but it gave him such a pain in the chest
that he wished he hadn’t swallowed a mouthful.
Then, naturally, he got so weak and nervous he
couldn’t sleep o’ nights. This, as everybody
knows, is the straight road to the graveyard,
through the madhouse. Lots of people are making
that trip all the time. We must eat and we must
sleep. If we don’t we are done for. Neuralgic
pains, too, made matters worse for Mr. Strong.
He saw a doctor, and what did the doctor do?
This is what the patient says on that point :
“ This doctor gave me all hinds of strengthening
medicines, but none of them did me any good, and
I continued to suffer for month after month.”
Just what we might have expected. Mr. Strong
further says : “In October, 1890, my friend, Mr.
James Webster, of 28, New Walk, Leicester,
advised me to try a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup, and I followed his advice. The
first bottle gave me great relief, and I began to
digest my food and to have an appetite ; and after
I had used three bottles I was quite another man.
I was completely cured. All my aches and pains
left me, my strength returned, and I have been
all right ever since. What astonished me was
that the Syrup cured me so quickly, and I shall
never cease thanking Mr. Webster for making it
known to me. You are at liberty to publish my
caseforthebenefit of others. Yours truly, (Signed)
W. Strong, 41, East Street, Leicester, December
30th, 1891.”
Now, was there a mistake in this matter, and
if so, what was it ? Yes, there was a common
mistake made. It is an old and seemingly hope-
less blunder.
“The doctor,” says Mr. Strong, “gave me all
kinds of strengthening medicines .”
Open wide your ears and remember what I’m
going to tell yon now ; remember it for the hour
of your own helplessness and pain; There is no
such thing as strengthening medicine, neither in the
earth nor in the waters which are under the earth.
That is the truth; all the high-class doctors know
it. As for the others, well there ! the less said
about them the better.
Mr. Strong took “strengthening medicines,”
and what happened to him? Why, he “continued
to suffer month after month.” Did you ever see
a horse made stronger and fatter by spurs and
lash-whips, leaving off the oats and hay ? I think
you have not. And that is what “ strengthening
medicines ” do, and all they do. So-called tonics
are like making a sick man walk far and fast by
kicking him at every other step.
Nothing under the sun but digested food imparts
strength ; no drug ever does.
And herein is the perpetual success and victory of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup ; it cleanses the system of
disease-poison and leaves Nature sweet and free.
The stomach then cries, “ Feed me,” and so power
and health come back like the green grass after a
shower. Do yon see? Mother Seigel proclaims
“ I destroy disease ! ” Nature responds, “ Only
do that, and I can take care of myself.”
The disease is indigestion and dyspepsia. Mr.
Strong had it and was badly treated. Should you
have it, try the Syrup first—not last, for I have
told you why. Experience.
London, March, 1892.
A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone knows what a good stove is—at least,
the women do ; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really fir.-1-
rate, reliable cooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house. A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses—thrown together to sell A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it does not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are
1. Strong cast-iron frame.
2. Good draught.
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
breakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that is also an ornament to the kitchen,
all the better.
Price, of course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con.
fidence, we can honestly recommend “The
Housewife,” sold by Holdsworth, Macplwrson
and Co., 250 George-street, Sydney, from ,£3 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely “ fills the
bill.”
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
particulars.

Sud-Est (New Guinea) natives are now just as
smart prospectors as the white diggers, and hold on to
their gold, too, until they truck it at £3 10s. an oz. The
whites used to hire the niggers at 6d. a day ; but this is
played out, and the blackfellow works for himself and
deals direct with the storekeeper.

Owing it is said, to the unexpected rush of
applications it hag been resolved to close the share-list of
the Woggonora Building Society’s property distribution
on May 25 a month earlier than originally intended. The
prizes consist of 60,000 acres of good land in the imme-
diate vicinity of Cunnamulla (Q.), 20,000 sheep, and all
the necessary buildings, dams, fences, tanks, and other
appurtenances of three excellent grazing farms. The
first prize is valued at £6500 ; the second at £5500 ; and
the third at £4500. Also, there are 171 smaller prizes
and the winner of any one of these has the option of
selling his prize back to the society for 90 per cent, of its
nominal value in cash. This is a substantial guarantee
that the values are in no way inflated. Further particulars
in our advertising columns.

The Jerger gold-saving machine appears to be
making its wav. What is claimed for it is that it is cheap
—the price is only £l5, delivered at Sydney-and port-
able that it can tackle almost any kind of wash, that it
puts through 400 buckets daily with a small amount of
water, and that it comes nearer to the ideal gold-saver—
the machine which allows nothing whatever to escape it—
than any other yet invented. And if it approaches any-
where within gunshot of the maker’s expectations in these
respects it will be a boon such as has never before come in
the way of the digger.
The New York Spectator., a financial
paper of high repute and long standing, publishes
a summary of the business of the great life offices
of the United States for 1893. Its tables are a
brilliant advertisement for the great New York
Life Insurance Co. Taking the three largest
offices, the Mutual has assets amounting to
184,930,000 dollars; the Equitable owns 164,701,000
dollars; and the New York Life 148,<00,000
dollars; but while the State Insurance Depart-
ment made considerable deductions from the
figures of the first two —no less than 4j millions of
the Equitable’s assets being of a kind which the
department refused to recognise—the New York
Go.’s assets were passed by the State auditor
as absolutely correct. The New York Life
Co. shows a surplus of 17,000,000 dollars over
all liabilities, against the Mutual’s surplus of
only 16,000,000 dollars on a considerably larger
business. The New York Co. lost much less than
half as much business by surrenders as did the
Mutual, and a shade more than half the amount
lost by the Equitable, thus showing that its con-
nection is of a stable character, and its policy-
holders have confidence in it and have come to j
stay. The New York Life issued new policies, in
1893, to the amount of nearly 224,000,000 dollars,
being nearly forty millions more than either of
its rivals. In 1893, the New York Co.’s new
insurance was 53,000,000 dollars more than its
new business for 1892; that of the Mutual
not quite 42,000,000 dollars more ; and
that of the Equitable not quite ha(f*a-million
dollars more. The New York Oo.’s premium
income from its new business for 1893
was 1,682,698 dollars more than that of the
previous year; while the Mutual’s was only
421,415 dollars more, and the Equitable’s 41,387
dollars more. The New York Co. had also by far
the largest increase of insurance in force, but while
it stood at the top in all these respects, in the
matter of increased expenses it stands at the very
bottom. With all its immense expansion of busi-
ness its expenses for the year were only 677,000
dollars more than those of 1892, while the Equit-
able’s expenses rose by 699,000 dollars, and those
of the Mutual by over two million dollars. These
are points which all insurers should look at. The
office which loses least by surrenders, and does
the largest new business at the smallest expense
has a great deal to recommend it.

In the way of concentrated nourishment
there is nothing on this earth which is quite so
concentrated as Bovril. It is condensed bull it
is ox in the smallest imaginable compass—the
strongest and most abbreviated form of beef on
record —and it represents all the benefits and ad-
vantages of the he-cow in the narrowest possible
space. Bovril, made into a drink after the man-
ner of beef-tea, is a combination of food and
medicine, and it can also be used instead of butter
or as an adjunct thereto, instead of the tasteless,
antediluvian sandwich. Also, it can be employed
in making soup, gravy, or, in fact, anything
wherein the ordinary ox is a customary ingredient.
CITY MUTUAL LIFE OFFICES, HUNTER, BLIGH AND CASTLEREAGH STREETS, SYDNEY.

nourishing than any other preparation of meat,
and it looks like it. |

drapers of George-street, Sydney, have resolved

during the 1894 season. The footballer s great
desire is to wear the loudest club costume on
earth, and the firm keeps 29 different kinds of
costume in stock to suit 29 different varieties of
footballer. Any football costume which isn t kept
in stock will be supplied in any quantity, and in
colours that are calculated to make the bird ot
paradise fall down dead, inside of 14 days. All
other kinds of drapery belonging to that game
which consists in the breathless pursuit of blown
leather are also on hand, also all drapery apper-
taining to every other kind of game ; and the calf -
and-breastbone brigade in general is cordially
invited to o»U.
In these days of depression one naturally
looks for an investment that requires but little
capital, with a prospect of acquiring a handsome
return. There are few opportunities, however,
for those with limited means to become possessed
of a good sheep-station, with house and valuable
improvements, besides 6000or7000first-class young
sheep from which to derive a really good yearly in-
come. This opportunity is now offered for .£1 by
investing it in a share in the Woggonora Building
Society, as the shareholders intend dividing
amongst themselves three grazing farms of 20,000
acres each, together with 20,000 sheep and J635C0
worth of freehold property in 171 lots, which is
readily saleable at 90 per cent, of its stated value.
The distribution is to take place next month, so
now’s the time to chance £1 in a society conducted
under Queensland Government supervision.*
A Sydney firm is printing the Gospel of
St. Mark in two New Guinea dialects. The words
are simply spelled phonetically, the vowels re-
ceiving the Italian sound. It looks one of the
ugliest languages ever set in type.
Last year’s financial crisis has perhaps
more than anything else brought the life offices
of these colonies prominently before the public.
The manner in which they have stood by their
policyholders merits all commendation, in the
van stood that sterling little Sydney institution,
The City Mutual Life, which, during the past hve
years, has by the application of energy ana
economy, assisted by more than the average busi-
ness ability, steadily emerged from obscurity
into the front rank. Mr. Crowley seized the
opportunity to notify his policyholders that
those of them affected by the then prevailing
suspensions could on application have the grace
on their policies extended without charge, and the
act, trifling as it was, bore fruit a thousand-told.
The City Mutual did not hesitate either to put.
its hand pretty deeply into its pocket to assist its
needy policyholders in a practical way, and the
1 crowning act was the intimation, some six month
ago, through every paper in the colony, that any
member who had paid three years premiums
could obtain a fully-paid policy equal to the whole
amount of premiums paid with compound interest
added. Such treatment goes far to justify the
rather ambitious title which Mr. Crowley has
patented for his office—the most liberal and pro-
gressive Life office in Australia. The Society
removed into its handsome new building—of
which we give a picture—at the intersection
of Hunter, Bligh and Castlereagh Streets in
January last.*
Cabled that 100,000 negroes are to leave
the States for Liberia, West Africa—“ going
home.” The Queensland nig. goes “home’ one
by one, with a kick, a curse, and a shovelful of
earth atop—but he gets there just the same
Mr. Leopold Yates, erstwhile P.M. at
Yass, N.S.W., and afterwards at Sydney, is now
a very great swell indeed at Nice, and a regular
habitue of the card-tables at the Oercle de la
Mediterranee.
A Bulletin correspondent puts another
face on Parkes’ Westralian welcome “ The
crowd only came to see him because he has been
so well advertised in The Bulletin. The impres-
sion prevails that he doesn’t deserve the advertise-
ment. We all admired him—till we heard him
talk.”
Q. capitalistic journals do their best to
account for the Ipswich election. One says the
railway-workshop men were enraged at retrench-
ment; another that Stephenson’s party squabbled.
But the worst excuse of all is that which says the
Ipswich Fat Man only reaps what he has sown,
since he so consistently damned MTlwraith in
’BB, before the coalition, that the voters have
neyer forgotten it. The curses of the Barlow-
Stephenson crowd, in fact, have come home to
roost. All this is delightful Sunday reading for
Wilkinson, M.P., and the Labour-party.

Professor Thulinius.— My Dear Sir,—l feel I should be want-
ing- in duty, did I neglect the opportunity of sending you my
testimony as to the wondrous efficacy of your rheumatic
specific. For the past ten years 1 have been a complete
martyr to rheumatism in all its forms. I have tried doctors
. . . . with a view of effecting a cure for this most trying
disease I am well-known, and scores can testify
that at best I could only crawl about with difficulty. . .
After leaving Little Bay Hospital at the close of last year I
was persuaded to try a bottle of your " Thulinius," and in a
week or so afterwards I could get about fairly well. Upon
again suffering a relapse last February, I took another bottle,
with an equally successful result; but at last I determined to
go through a course of treatment, and I have just completed
my fourth bottle, with the gratifying knowledge that I am
quite free from pain, can walk any reasonable distance with-
out fatigue, have an excellent appetite, and feel myself again
competent to re-enter my profession, added to which my oft-
repeated attacks of pleurisy have become a thing of the past.
I have not dieted myself in any way, so all the credit is due to
your wonderful remedy, under Providence, that my blood is
becoming purer daily, and its effect upon my liver and kid-
neys most pronounced. ... I shall only be too glad
personally to convince fellow-sufferers of the benefits to be
derived from a course of your treatment, as exemplified in
my own case, after ten years’ martyrdom.—l remain, yours
faithfully, VV. H. NEWTON NEWMAN (Teacher of Elocution
and twenty-five years Actor and Manager),
[The Rev. J.D. Langley, St. Philip’s Parsonage, and Mr. H. H.
Lusk, Barrister-at-Law, can testify as to the crippled state of
Mr. Newman prior to his taking “ Thulinius.”]

In the back-blocks of Bananaland, two
stockmen were riding along a bush-track, some
years Said one to the other, as he leisurely
cut a pipe of tobacco, “ I can back my horse to
find every stump there is and stumble over it.
See that little stump ahead, not six inches out of
the ground? I’ll bet he trips over it.” The
stump was not quite directly ahead, but the horse
stumbled over it, all the same, and his rider,
whose hands were engaged with his tobacco and
knife, was pitched out of the saddle and broke his
neck.
While the smellful Chinaman and the per-
functory supervision of lepers usual to Australia
continue to exist, the mortality from leprosy must
Articles miles long have been written by
speculative medical men proving to the writers’
own satisfaction that leprosy is possible by inocu-
lation, and, on the other hand, that it isn’t, but
few practicable remedies have been suggested.
One of these few, judging by the testimonials
received, is that advanced by the Orange Butter Co.
1 he co. is the sole Australasian agent for a blood
and skin medicine compounded by Dr. Koch, of
consumption-cure celebrity, which is claimed to
have a direct influence on those diseases to which
it is especially applicable. The medical fraternity
say that leprosy is not curable, the agents of Dr.
Koch’s remedy assert positively that it is.
Elsewhere in this issue is advertised the
financial statement of the Mutual Life Insurance
Company of New York for the year 1893. There
are ® good many big commercial institutions in
the States but this is the biggest. At the close of
last year its assets reached the useful sum of
£38,338,332 13s. 7d. sterling. This would be a
considerable fund even if the 7d. were missing.
Having set apart <£34,651,965 6s. lid. to meet
policies as they fall in, and other liabilities, the
managers find themselves the happy possessors of
a surplus of .£3,686,367 6s. Bd. which stands
as profits for distribution. This is alarger surplus
by <£571,740 4s. lOd. than was held at the
end of the previous year, and sets a good example
in the way of surpluses to Colonial Treasurers.
The Company’s_ income for 1893 was .£8,614,609
19s. 5d., and its disbursements, for the same
period, <£6,236,147 16s. 4d. Insurances and
annuities assumed and renewed, rejecting all un-
completed transactions, mount up to .£145,522,084
13s. Bd. I igures like these are startling results of
half a century s development on lines purely co-
operative and mutual, not a penny of capital
being planked at the start outside of premiums
paid. But then the managers of the concern have
never disputed or quarrelled. They have just run
the business in a sensible, capable fashion.
THE JOHN JERGER GOLD-SAVING
MACHINE.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Linigar, of Harden, in the colony of
New South Wales, hereby solemnly and sincerely
declare as follows That I placed in the John
Jerger Gold-saving Machine three pennyweights
of fine gold in one dish of washdirt, and after
working the machine for about three minutes
recovered the whole of the gold. This test was
made in the presence of two experienced miners
(Mr. Atkinson and Mr. Siggs) and several other
persons interested in mining. 1 make this declara-
tion unsolicited and simply in the interests of the
public generally, believing that the machine will
prove of inestimable benefit to the colonies of
Australasia.
William Linigar.

day of March, 1894, before me—Wm. Carson,

Hokianga (M.L.j possesses an artful Maori,
who engaged to work off at sheep-shearing an old
debt he owed a fanner. Honi went out on strike
early in the day, and, when asked why, replied
that he was hungry, and “an empty sack could
nut stand whereupon the farmer invited him to
dinner. The Maori’s capacities were big, but he
evidently got supplied, for, on his return to the
shed, the farmer asked him why he was standing
up, and not shearing ? Replied Honi: “You fill a
sack with potatoes, and how can you expect it to
bend ?”

Modern Dentistry.
Artificial teeth are almost as old as civilisation
itself. Possibly they are quite as old and were
themselves the beginning of civilisation. The
teeth have always been the weakest point in the
human anatomy, and probably those of primeval
man were no better than those of his remote
descendants. Therefore, as the winter of his dis-
content came upon him, and his years were many
in the land, he became a haggard and gummy
savage presenting a toothless front to the
storms of adversity. When he passed from
this state of primitive barbarism the first
thing he did was to make teeth. No one
knows when he made his first experiments
in this direction, but artificial teeth have been
found among the dust of the oldest Pharaohs who
lie beneath forgotten pyramids beside the waters
of the Nile, and probably enough that very, very
ancient monarch who smiled upon Joseph, smiled
with a complete set of bought teeth not so very
much unlike those of the present day. There were
shop teeth also among the Tyrians, Carthaginians,
Sidonians, and other nations without number, and
the art of making them flourished till the fall of
the Byzantine empire. Then it was lost amid the
general wreck like innumerable other arts, and
man became again, in his latter years, the same
toothless ruin as before. A solemn and portentous
being was mediaeval man, as he mumbled abjectly
on his way to a grave which closed over him
prematurely because of the indigestion which he
acquired through bolting his food in the Dark
Ages. The resurrection of dentistry within recent
times has added many years to the average dura-
tion of life. But it has been resurrected on a
new and entirely different plan. The artificial
teeth which are made to-day are not so very much
unlike those that were manufactured in the days
of Raineses the Great, but whereas the latter cost
a king’s ransom, even in the days when kings
were worth ransoming, the latter are within the
reach of everybody, and even the poorest need not
be without them. They have grown cheaper and
better, year after year, until at last the London
Dental Institute (of 98 King-street, Sydney), can
supply an excellent and durable set for 21s.
and single teeth from 2s. 6d. upwards. These are
probably the very lowest prices upon record, and
even at these figures a guarantee for five years is
given. The processes are entirely painless, and
the work is quite as beautiful as the best original
product of nature. Also, it has many advan-
tages which the product of nature never possessed.
There is no toothache, no decay, no hollow
places, none of the general misery which attaches
to the teeth which were made by a far seeing Pro-
vidence. There are no casual monstrosities in the
way of odd fangs which stand up above the rest like
solitary peaks in a desert. They are a good colour,
while the natural teeth, as often as not, are yellow
or of miscellaneous hues. And, as already men-
tioned, the London Dental Institute guarantees
them for five years, which neither Nature nor any
other dentist will do. This firm has establish-
ments in Melbourne, Sydney, Adelaide, and Bris-
bane, and is working a revolution in all four
capitals.
A Scotch paper, the Glasgow Herald,
publishes a yarn about the finding somewhere on
the Thompson (Q.) of a man who had died of
thirst. One of the deceased’s bony hands clutched
a Bible, the other a photograph of Gladstone.
The story seems painfully familiar somehow,
barring the photo, of Gladstone. It used to be
the portrait of Queen Victoria.
Dear Bulletin, — “Extremes meet” —in
mateship as in marriage. I remember a curious
instance of the David and Jonathan business in
the “good old days” at Stawell (Vic.). Jim
M , a “Geordie” —a magnificent six-foot of
manhood, slow and heavy of speech—a ruminating
animal, and Tom G , a “ Cousin Jack ” (un-
usual match)—five-foot nothing, and as mercurial
as a monkey—-were mates. They worked together
on sinking contracts, drank together, and cursed
and swore at each other most amicably. They
had a contract to sink a shaft a second hundred
feet. The price was good, a new “ whip ” erected,
the horse staunch, and the boy driver about as
cheeky as you make ’em. One day the mates had
just finished drilling, and had knocked for the
rope to be sent down. The boy led his horse down
the whip track, but in turning the horse, managed
to let go the heavy hook at the rope end, and down
the shaft rattled the whole concern, Warned by
the row, the big fellow, ostrich-like, hid his head
in the nearest corner of the shaft—while “ Cousin,”
with one agile spring, got underneath him. The
iron hook luckily landed without touching Jim
M , the wily Thomas it didn’t reach. In
their cups, Jim would often reproach the “little
’un ” that he didn’t care if he (Jim) was killed,
so that his own carcase was safe. “Goeston,
booye,” “Cousin” would retort, “thickey wur
brains agen blubber—what praichers call th’
triumph of Mind over Matter.” — “The Palmer. V
We are informed that the directors of the
A.M.P. Society have recently had under considera-
tion the rate of interest charged to members of the
society on loans on security of their policies.
Hitherto the rate has been 7 per cent., which does
not seem excessive considering the terms under
which such advances have been made. Loans
have been granted from upwards without any
legal or other expenses, and permission has. been
given to repay either in whole or by instalments, at
any time at the option of the borrower. It has
been felt, however, that in cases where members
were entitled to borrow large sums a lower
rate might be conceded, provided the loans were
taken for a fixed term and subject to the usual
conditions of mortgages on real estate, and it was
therefore resolved to grant sums of =£2oo or more
for a period of not less than two years at the lower
rate of 6 per cent, provided more than 75 per cent,
of the value of the policy were not required—the
interest in such cases to be paid quarterly. It
still remains optional, hpwever, with members to
borrow to the higher limit of 90 per cent, of the
value, and retain the privilege of paying off at any
time, either by instalments or in whole should they
prefer to do so, at. the old rate of 7 per cent.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Melbourne Oup stakes for 1894, instead of
being £lO,OOO and a trophy, will be only £5OOO and no
trophy. This fearsome writing on the wall appeared last
Tuesday, and till then no one had even begun to realise
the true awfulness of Melbourne’s fall. And since then
words have failed to express the situation, and the British
language has filed a feeble and anguished schedule in
despair. Ichabodl Melbourne the great is fallen—is
fallen I The grass withereth ; the flower fadeth ; and I lie
Cup has gone bung. Only the eloquent Jeremiah, the pro-
phet of desolation, could do justice to the situation, and
therefore The Bulletin hereby presents its readers with a
few extracts from that weeping prognosticator of old, as
its humble tribute in a day of great calamity. And on
second thoughts, it doesn’t present the aforesaid ex-
tracts, because there is nothing even in Jeremiah that is
quite desolate enough to meet the case. So Tub Bulletin
heaps ashes on its head—or at least it would do so only
the engine is run by gas and there aren’t any ashes on the
premises—and cries incoherently with a loud voice saying
nothing in particular, but thinking several things.

The eight oarsmen to represent N.S.W. against
Victoria have been finally chosen. Natty M'Donald is
emergency-man 1 Good old emergency 1 Natty would be
just getting warm when half of the crew as at present
constituted were rowed out.
» • • •
The Victorian intercolonial eight finally chosen
will consist of the two Donalds and Maher (Wendouree),
Charnley and Horshrugh (Yarra), Davies and Lindgren
(Mercantile), with Sloan (Electric Telegraph) and Begg
(Melbourne) as emergencies. With Enticott (Yarra) and
Dr. Kenny (Melb.), the crew would probably be improved,
and, on the whole, it is not a bit better than last year’s,
which proved a soft thing for the Sydneyites.
* * • •
W. H. Davies, one of the chosen Vie. eight,
drops out, dissatisfied with his seat, so rumour says.
The bow side of the Vic. boat, says a correspondent, is
strong enough to pull the strokes around in circles.
Another glorious defeat looms ahead for the Southerners.
• * • •
Many of the leading Sydney metallicians declare
“they will not put on the bag” at any more pony and
galloway race meetings. Only a few weeks ago there were
several numbers hoisted on the board for one race. Some-
body connected with the favourite ofliced it to a
book, to “put in the bag 1” “Not me!” replied the
astute one, “ I want to lay agin live ’uns. It’s the dead
’un* which sends us home broke.” The supposed ‘‘stiff "
won after all 1 Are the Tommies to be blamed if they
kick against such a system of barefaced robbery 7

For Autumn Rosehill meeting there are more
unlikely things than that the grey filly, Bliss, may win the
first end of the big double. Nightingale, Division, and
The Trier may be found in the first batch in the struggle
for the other end.

The sporting gaffer again. Fitzsimmons says he
is now stonebroke, after winning £16,000 in purses alone.
In future, Fitz. declares, he will do his own management.

The N.S.W. Cyclists Union elected Gov. Duff
the patron of last year, but denounces him because he will
not pay five guineas this year. Which looks very like
blackmail.

Only three horses finished in the Steeplechase
at Randwick on Saturday, and the inevitable poor devil
of a jockey was ambulanced to St. Vincent’s Hospital.
Hiwever, The Bulletin is glad to notice that such things
don’t shock the ladies very much. The modern fashion-
able woman apparently thinks no more of seeing a man
crushed and carried off the course thau the old-timer did
of turning down her thumbs.

On the same night that some Sydney policemen
last raided a miserable Chinese fan-tan shop. Gov. Dull
had “to dinner ” several of the most prominent turf-
sportsmen in N.S.W. The amount involved in an ordinary
race-meeting’s transactions of one of these guests would
buy up ail the Chinese gambling-dens in the Sydney
metropolitan area.
* * * *
The battle of the books and horses at Auckland
(M.L.) University has resulted in victory for the horses.
Prof. Aldis wanted to kick Teddy O’Rorke’s pony out of
the college stables. But Teddy’s father was chairman of
the College Council, and put a large, flat foot down.
Hence the biggest educational row that Maoriland has
seen, ending in the retirement of the professor.
* » * *
Village Maid is reported to have cleared 38ft.
in the Hurdle Race at Cooriejong. This is close to
Chandler’s 39ft.

are more formidable than the Flemington fences.

Year by year the genuine country turf clubs are
going down while metropolitan swindles flourish. The

Melb. cyclists met in full force last Saturday
afternoon to protest against the £5-duty. The demon-
stration, which was run by the importers, was fairly suc-
cessful as a show ; but, although no machines are made in
Victoria, the fact remains that an Adelaide-made “ bike"
has won an Austral; also, that Tom’s record-ride from
the saintly city to Melb. was also won on a local machine.

The ambition of Mick MTnerny, the Melb.
footballer and boxer, was checked on Saturday night by
his seven-round defeat by Tom Duggan, at the Cumber-
land Club. Duggan, who now poses as middle-weight
champion, has been beaten but once—by a chance blow
from a man whom he knocked down half-a-dozen times in
the first round.
• • ♦ •
Larry Foley predicts a Sydney boxing boom in
the near future. As of yore, the champions will come
back stone-broke, and, availing himself of cheap talent,
the only fistic caterer can then give the public a good
bill-of-fare.

The amateur reviewer of a N.S.W, country
paper was sent a copy of Miss Braddon’s “ The
Dav Will Come” for a notice.’ It panned out
thusly : “ We have received, from Mr. Drury,
oLtioner, &c., of this town, a copy of a work by
Miss Braddon, entitled, ‘ The Day Will Come.’
We may remark that so will the night.”
At a Sydney R.O. Church, on a recent
a n( jav the cashiers and bookkeepers had just
begun to take up a collection, starting at the end
next the pulpit. The holy father heard the first
fa W coins drop in and they had a coppery sound
which suggested poverty. Suddenly he lifted up
a short, admonitory finger and said Stop !
The collection stopped right there, and the father,
with thunder on his brow, stepped down and
counted the takings for the first three seats. Then
i, e resumed his position, and in the course of five
minutes he hurled every imaginable variety of fire
and brimstone at the flock, and uncorked the
flames of hell generally. When he had finished
the collection went on again, leaving the
rlplinauenta in the first three seats stunned and
nearly insensible.
What Melbourne papers apparently regard
an exceedingly humorous case was lately settled
in the local police court. Two men fought for the
nossession of a particular “doss,” the corner of a
dust-bin near the wharf. Buchanan claimed that
the doss was his by precedence, and finding Shaw
in possession when about to retire one evening, he
hauled him out, and stated his claim. In the
“ scrap ” that ensued Shaw was handicapped by
the absence of his trousers which were being
mended by a mate at the nearest lamp-post. The
Bench held that Buchanan was legally justified in
the action he had taken-on the principle that a
Briton’s “doss” is his castle, apparently—but
thought he was wanting in hospitality in turning
out a trouserless man on a cold and censorious
world. Case dismissed. These men were not
loafers. It is a good joke in a young country to
have working-men fighting for a kennel to creep
into.
THE AVERAGE MAN.
His hat looks worn and his coat-sleeves shine
As I see him step from his ’bus at nine,
His boots are pieced and his tie home-made,
And his trousers patched where the edge was frayed,
And his face is lined by the stress of Life
Where a man must fight for his bairns and wife.
“ Who’s that?” I ask, as his face I scan.
And the answer comes: “ Oh ! an average Man 1”
He has not got notes, he has not got gold, ,
But his homely lunch, in his handbag old.
And day by day as the seasons go
He follows'his duty to and fro.
And shadows follow him everywhere—
Grim Want and Worry and Dread are there.
For Life is nut on a gorgeous plan —
Far, far from it—to the average man.
The Floods, the Banks, and the curtailed screw,
The weekly bills and the grasping Jew,
The servant’s wage and the Doctor's fee,
And the needful change by the breezy sea,
And the pent-up hours at the desk which mean
A man’s brain changed to a mere machine,
And a wife’s tired eyes and the children wan,
All press like lead on the average man.
When the blood is up ’tis a simple thing
To charge where the bombs and the bullets sing!
But he is worthy of a higher place
Who fronts his woes with a smiling face.
For the noblest strife in our Life to-day
Is the humdrum fight in the humdrum way.
O Wealth and Genius may lead the van !
But the hero is often—an average man !
G. ESSEX EVANS
-in the
Queenslander.
Hypnotism is so far advanced that you can
cram a student for examination hypnotically, and
he will remember it all when he wakes up. So
much advantage has been taken of this dodge by
examinees in France that it is now forbidden by
law.
London Graphic advertisement:—
QOOK— Can any Lady Recommend a reliable Good
. Plain Cook for the country, where assistance is

land ;no fringe. Address, Box 150, Daily Telegraph
office, 194 Strand, W.C.
“No fringe.” The fringe is a sign of worldliness
and frivolity, and no really serious English family
would have a servant whose hair wasn’t plastered
down flat on her head, like the greasy locks of a
footballer. To all intents and purposes the
British creed is that no fringe shall enter the
kingdom of Heaven, and that only the very plain
person of the “lower orders” can hope to be
saved. Half the feminine charitable societies in
England really exist for the purpose of sternly
admonishing the young person of the lower orders
who isn’t so plain as she ought to be. Thus
London Referee: —
Without being ungallant, I cannot help urging that
women in a position of authority are harder-hearted and
less considerate than men ; and, in spite of the fact that
women have of late years become the dominant sex, they
still remain small-minded in small matters. No man would
work himself up into a state of absolute fury because his
male servant went out on Sunday in a hat the same shape
as his own, but who that has had domestic experience
cannot recall the flush of anger on the face of the mistress
of the house if Mary Jane is observed taking her departure
on Sunday afternoon in a hat and feathers or a mantle of
fashionable shape ? No master would object to his man-
servant parting his hair either in the middle or at the
side, but it is not so long since we had a furious attack on
the “fringe” of the female servant carried on by indig-
nant mistresses in the public press. Why, because a girl
takes the honourable and onerous position of domestic
servant, she should be compelled to wear her hair just as
it pleases her mistress to decide, I have never been able
to understand; and it is also a mystery to me why she
should not be allowed to dress exactly as she chooses
when she is not on duty.
One of the features of N.S.W. Agricul-
tural Society’s show for 1894 was the splendid
exhibits of butter sent in by the Fresh Food and
Ice Co. This company exhibited in only two
sections and was awarded first prize in each, and
the champion prize. Its butter is known far and
wide as the very best in Australia, and its brand,
when affixed to the finished product of the cow,
is a sufficient guarantee of excellence. The
“ Golden Star ” brand of creamery butter, which
gained champion prize at the aforesaid show, is
especially well-known. It is delivered in 11b, and
Jib. pats, and is wrapped up in a special vegetable
parchment which is impervious to grease, so
that the purchaser of a sample package may
carry it away in his bag without soiling or
injury. The company’s “Moss Rose” brand of
prime factory butter is almost equally famous.
One cause of the excellence of the Fresh
Food and Ice Co.’s product is the employ-
ment of a special machine for moulding
and printing the pats. Other causes which make
the “Golden Star’ and the “Moss Rose” the
most favourite brands in the market are the ex-
ceeding cleanliness with which all the operations
of manufacture are conducted, the skill of the
operators, and the use of the best and most
modern appliances. The Fresh Food and Ice Co.
also supplies milk, cream, fresh fish—Australian,
English, and from Maoriland; and delivers its
goods everywhere in Sydney and suburbs, and in
all the outlying parts of N.S. Wales where rail-
-1 way, steamer or coach communication is available.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DU ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.
