“At the Feet of His Fame."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I.
“ What is your name, please?”
“ Jane Marson.”
“Tell the jury, please, what you are and what
you know of the persons into the cause of whose
deaths we are now inquiring.”
“I was servant to Mr. and Mrs. Baird ”
“ Their only servant! ”
“ Their only servant. I had been with them
since their marriage, four years ago about. Yester-
day ”
“ Well?”
“ I found them both —dead ! ”
“ Describe, please—take your time!—how you
found them.”
“I got up about seven o’clock and began my
housework as usual. I first brought the papers in
from the verandah, and looked to see whether the
House had sat late ”
“Never mind that. Tell us how you found
the bodies.”
“ I am coming to that. You see, sir, if the
House was sitting late I put breakfast off, and did
not take the papers up to Mrs. Baird’s room. I
saw the House had not sat late, so I was taking
the papers upstairs when
“What?”
“ I had to pass the room in which Mr, Baird
used to keep his books and papers and do his
writing. The door was open, and I was surprised
to see him sitting at the table. The blinds were
down, and the lamp, which was still burning, was
dim, so, at first, 1 thought him asleep—fallen
asleep over his reading,”
“ What did you do?”
“I did not disturb him—l thought he would
not like it, so I went up to their bedroom intend-
ing to awake Mrs. Baird so that she might rouse
him. I knocked at the room-door, but got no
answer, so went in and drew up the blinds. Then
I went to the bed and touched Mrs. Baird to wake
her. She was dead.”
“ Describe how you found her.”
“ She was lying in bed, just as if she was asleep
—just as she has been lying at other times when
I have gone in when Mr. Baird has been away
from home,”
“Were there any signs of disturbance in her
dress—the bed-clothes ?”
“ Were neat and uncrumpled—at least, nothing
more than ordinary—now I think of it, not so
much. ”
“And she was dead ?”
“ Quite dead, sir—and cold !”
“What did you do then?”
“ I rushed downstairs to Mr Baird’s room ”
“ His study ?’’
“They did not call it a study. They called it
the ‘Den.’ And when Mrs. Baird was in a
teasing humour she would speak of it as “The
Bear’s Den.”
“Ah ! And you went to the * Den ?’ ”
“ Yes—to rouse Mr. Baird ! I hardly knew
what I was doing—l was so startled, but that was
my idea, I know !’’
“ And you found——?”
“That he was dead, too. I remember now I
put my hand on his shoulder ”
“ Was he sitting upright ?”
“ No, his head was bent on his clasped hands,
which rested upon the table. I called him, and
shook him, and then I knew he was dead.”
“What did you do, then?’*
“ I don’t know—for some time I was stupid,
dazed. ’
“Did you faint?”
“ I think I did, sir. After a time, I went to
the neighbours, and told them. They sent for the
police—who have had charge of the house since.”
There was a long pause. Then the coroner
asked, with a strident emphasis—
“ What did you do with the chloral bottle —or
bottles, witness?”
“ The—chloral, sir?” The witness was confused
16 rs *' * ; ' me during her examination.
“Yes, the chloral. The medical testimony is
that death in both cases resulted from chloral-
poisoning ?”
. *’ It was overdoses, sir, overdoses—l’m sure of
it!”
“Ah, how can you be sure of it? But we’ll
come back to that again. Where are the chloral
bottles ?”
No answer. The question was repeated sternly.
Still the woman’s lips were closed.
“ I warn you that you are placing yourself in a
very serious position, witness ! The presumption
is that you removed the bottle or bottles of chloral
before you notified the neighbours. Did you ?”
Quiveringly she answered at last: “Y-e-es!
But I’m sure they both took overdoses V 9
“To what place did you remove the drug ?”
“ I’ll go and get them, sir—l will, sir !”
Were there two bottles, or was there only
one?”
“ Two.”
“ Where were they when you first saw them ?”
One on the little table by Mrs. Baird’s bed—
the other in his hands.”
“You removed them before vou went for the
neighbours ?”
The witness nodded.
“ Now—where to?”
“ To—my—box,”
“ At the Bairds’ house?”
“ Yes—l’ll go and get them, sir !”
“An officer will go with you. Sergeant .”
“Oh, never mind, sir—l’ll really bring’em, I
will. The sergeant needn’t bother.”
“You are in the court’s hands, witness. The
officer must go with you, or alone. 1 can empower
him to search for the drug.”
“ I—will—go, sir !”
11.
“Before we resume, gentlemen, the examina-
tion of the witness Marson, the sergeant of police
will testify as to his finding the chloral.”
“My name is William Brady. lam a sergeant
of police attached to No. 33 Division. Yesterday
morning I was placed by Inspector Phillips in
charge of this enquiry. . . . Upon informa-
tion received from the doctors, I made search for
a chloral bottle or bottles. I was unsuccessful.
The previous witness, when interrogated by me,
denied having seen them. When ordered by you
just now, sir, I proceeded with the previous wit-
ness to tne Bairds’ house, and in the room occu-
pied by that witness found a locked box. The
witness Marson unlocked it, just lifted the lid,
put her hand in, drew out the two bottles I pro-
duce, and let the lid drop again and locked the
box instantly. Her hand was in the box less than
half-a-minute. From a rustling of paper, it
seemed as if she had taken the bottles out of a
wrapper. I asked her had she done so ; she denied
it. I insisted upon examining the box myself.
She flung the key through the window, which was
open, into the shrubbery. I have told off a eon-
stable to bring the box here, sir, and another to
search for the key. The box may or may not
contain farther evidence bearing upon the case—
the inquiry.”
“You did right, Sergeant Brady. Stand down
now. Jane Marson!”
“What is your objection to having your box
examined ?”
“Because—”
“ The Court is waiting. I must warn you that
I will not put up with any further trifling. You
have already sought to deceive the Court. Why
do you object? Were there any wrappers on the
bottles?”
“No, sir, none.”
“Then, why do you object?”
Sergeant Brady here whispered to the Coroner.
“ The Sergeant tells me the key is found. Ser-
geant, open the box ”
“Oh, sir, never mind. I’ll tell you why, since
you must know —-I — Mrs. Baird told me some-
time ago I was to —have some of the jewellery if
she died—and I took it yesterday—and placed it
in the box.”
“Sergeant, open the box !”
The witness Marson burst into tears.
11l
“Now, witness, no jewellery has been found in
that box. Only these two letters, one in an
envelope addressed ‘hid ward’ and the other un-
enclosed, but marked ‘Felicia,’ were lying upon
the top of your clothes ! Bearing in mind that
the deceased Mr. Baird’s Christian name was
Edward, and Mrs. Baird’s ‘Felicia,’ how do you
explain the presence of these letters in your box?’*
No answer.
“For some reason or other, witness, you are
endeavouring to deceive the Court. You have
made statements that are misleading, as well as
others that are directly false. I might commit
you for perjury—I might even cause your arrest
on suspicion of having murdered Mr. and Mrs.
Baird— ”
“ I murder them ! That is good! Why, I was
too fond of them both !”
“Then why seek to pervert the course of
Justice ? This lady and gentleman may have been
murdered.”
“No—it was an overdose to each !”
“That is the third time you have made that
statement. It implies a specific knowledge of
how they met their deaths. What do you mean?”
“ Oh,” with a sudden blaze into a pained wrath,
“if you must know, read the letters !”
“Before I read the letters I wish you to prove
they bear on the inquiry. You found the letters,
where ?”
“ The one in the envelope on Mrs. Baird’s table
under the chloral bottle, the other between Mr,
Baird’s fingers.”
“ Then you removed them at the same time that
you removed the chloral bottles ?”
A motion of assent.
“ Why ?”
No answer.
“Was that before you called in the neighbours,
or after ?”
“ Be—fore!”
IV.
“ The jury will please listen closely. This letter
is evidently written by the male deceased, and
the witness Marson has now proved that it was
taken from his hand :
Midnight.
Sweetheart,— To-night, at dinner, I made up my mind
to adjust finally our relations. How, I did not then see.
It was only during the course of a debate in the House
that the solution of our difficulties to which I have came
suggested itself. And that is, dearest—my death. 1 love
you so well that 1 can give up fame for you by giving up
life ; and j’et, somehow, I do not love you well enough to
subject myself to the daily torture of living with a woman
who has no sympathy with my toils, with my aims, with
my dreams—who stabs me daily with her desires to under-
stand me no less acutely than she wounds me with her
misunderstandings. We have tried now for four years to
get closer to one another—and yet we are never so close
os when we arrive at the point of mutual toleration. If I
loved you less, life with you would be easier. If you
loved me less you would be happier; but as with all
our constant friction our passion seems to become the
stronger—l love you immeasureably more than I did
when I courted you—l feel that the dawning of each
day, while it throws open a new treasury of affection,
in the same moment widens the current of bitternesses
that divides while uniting us. Men say 1 have insight,
but the reason of this severance in regard, this love in
hatred, defies attempts to discover it. All I know is, that,
while at all times your mere presence is a delight to me,
there are frequent moments when your voice is a blow,
your manner, even your caress, an outrage. Never
otherwise than loving and tender ; ever anxious to under-
stand me and to forestall my wants, there is in you some
fatal element of incapacity ; or, in myself, some dreadful
unreasonableness, some terrible craving that has its roots
in brutal perversity. And so, dearest, I think it best to
spoil your life no longer. The fault is not yours - but in
me—or the perverse nature of things. You will find my
poor affairs in order—my insurance policy is indefeasible,
even by suicide. And others beside you know that I am
a chloral drinker. The empty bottle, following upon an
excited night in Parliament, will be sufficient explanation
to the world. When you wake in the early morning and
miss me from your side, you will come down to the Den
and find this in my hand. Keep it to yourself, beloved—
and the sole explanation the world will receive will be
“an overdose of chloral.” To-morrow the town will give
a start of mild surprise—and in the next moment, forget
“ the rising public man.” Do you, sweetheart, forget
me, if not as soon, at least soon.— E.
“Here, then, gentlemen, we have an explanation
of Mr. Baird’s death—we cannot yet say whether
it is the true explanation. I now turn to the letter
found according to the witness Marson by the
dead lady’s bedside. This, unlike the other, is
enclosed in an envelope which I open. It runs
so :
10 o’clock.
My Darling Husband, —It was borne in upon me to-
night, after our difference at the dinner-table, that the
one way open to me to prove my love is, dear, to leave
you free to obey the impulse of your great talents. On
all hands I hear you spoken of with admiration, and the
very strength of the criticism you and your speeches call
forth, I am told, is a tribute to your prowess, though the
bitter words pain me like a blow. And it is this that con-
vinces me I should leave you. If 1 were cleverer or wiser
1 should, perhaps, understand better how it is, and why
it is, you are both praised and censured. But being what
I am, only a simple woman, who has no power but that
of loving, while I gloat over the praise, I resent the cen-
sure, and I feel it so. It must be one of those things
which you so often tell me I can’t understand. And it
seems to me, dearest, that is the root of all our discord—
that I can’t understand you, or your motives, or your
methods of work. I strive honestly to do so—l read, and
I try to think, but 1 cannot, do what 1 will, feel an inter-
est in these political things which interest you and fas-
cinate you. I would not for a moment suggest you are
wrong, but sometimes I fancy you do not see the differ-
ence between a man who has countless interests outside
of his home and a woman who has none. lam only a
woman, who can’t do anything except love, and pray, and
keep house, things with which men have no sympathy
that lasts long. Not that your love has faded, dearest.
No, no 1 1 am going into the Dark—but it is not dark
utterly, for your love is so strong that it will light me
across the grave. What I mean is that, to me, love is
supreme ; to you, your ambition is the one thing which
you would not part with. . . . But why should 1 write
thus? I don’t know—except it is—oh, dear, this heart
that will not beat much longer, yet beats quicker at the
thought?—that my words may induce you to make sure
of yourself, and of her, should you take another wife to

least, do you understand her I Bind not another weak-

no talent but that of loving to your greatness. Heaven
knows that I have striven to be worthy of you—to bring

And so, dear, thinking this way, I will no longer bar

“ That is the poor lady’s letter, gentlemen——”
“There is a postscript over the page, sir !” said
a juryman.
“Ah ! I did not see that. It completes the
evidence, gentlemen. Listen :
P.S.—I have taken the contents, 11 p.m., of one of your
chloral phials. Say, dearest, that I was in the habit of
taking the drug—only Jane will know that was not so. I
will leave this so that you will see it when you come from
the House. Be sure, dearest, and keep it to yourself.
The world must not know. And you, Ned, you will never
know how much I loved you I—Your F.
“Gentlemen, these letters sadly simplify our
duty.”
“I wish to ask the witness Marson one ques-
tion, Mr. Coroner,” said a juror. The witness was
recalled.
“ Did your master and mistress ever quarrel ?”
“ Mr, and Mrs. Baird never quarrelled outright.
He would say sharp words, but almost instantly
beg her forgiveness.”
“ How—can you remember an instance?”
“One night he was walking up and down the
room, looking as if his thoughts were far away.
She read out to him some paragraph from a paper
—a funny paragraph. He stopped in his walk —
and damned her. Her baby prattle, he said, had
made him lose grip of the finest thoughts he had
had for many-a-day. A few moments later he
craved her pardon.”
‘‘What a pair of fools!” muttered a juryman,
with pendulous fatness of cheeks and white-
vested corpulence of body.
“Fools!” repeated the witness. “Fools!
Who are you to think you understand? Why,
perhaps you thought I was committing perjury
for the love of it, and nob for the love of them !
Fools >”
PRICE WARUNG.
Scorn —The produce of withered lips.
Napoleon, like Goethe, was born in his
mother’s 18th year.
At a recent “ society ” wedding in
Auckland (M.L.) the “bride” said promptly
“ No.” He pa “ remonstrated,” but she still said
“No.” After an adjournment, the clergyman
said that owing to the bride’s indisposition the
wedding would not happen. “Indisposition” is
an excellent word.
A S.M. Herald correspondent notices that
fine, new Bowen (Q.) oranges appear on the tables
of Queensland-trading steamers, but none ever find
their way to Sydney or Brisbane. Bowen is
noted for its fruit, and there is certainly room to
establish a trade between the early Northern
growers and the Southern market; but at present
heavy freight and commission charges kill enter-
prise at the outset.
Skeletons.
( FOR THE BULLETIN .)
What a weird, mysterious-looking object is a
skeleton ! I don’t mean the kind you meet with
in a medico’s surgery, or a public museum, but
the disconnected, discoloured heap of bones across
which a prospector sometimes stumbles in the
gloom of the mighty forest. Not a year passes
but one or two of these mouldering frames are
brought to light. Then a bush coroner and a set
of jurymen assemble and try to find out who it
was and how it died. Their inquiries generally
end in total failure.
Many are lost, more than half are never found.
To note a few cases :
About ten years ago a mannamed Hunkin, travel-
ling to the Mount Victoria goldfields, Tasmania,
was put on the right track by another prospector.
They parted—for ever. Government men and
private parties were scouring the vicinity when a
rumour arose that Hunkin had cleared out to Vic-
toria. Searchers were immediately recalled, and
another ghastly heap will probably be bagged and
inquested some day.
A much longer time ago a married woman left
her home on the N.W. coast of Tasmania. She
never returned. When the search was at its
height, a relative of the lost unfortunate was
heard to remark: “Three days is quite long
enough to look for the old woman.” This
charitable assertion brought in all parties, and
her remains lie awaiting an inquest. Near the
same place a poor little skeleton was found, with
the remains of a child’s braces and some trousers-
buttons close by.
Whilst I was on the West Coast, a surveyor got
dosed at one of the poison-shops called a pub.
The last that was seen of him in a live condition
was one evening when he walked into a pros-
pectors’ camp and stared vacantly at the occu-
pants. They invited him to have a drink of tea ;
but, with a yell, he rushed from them into the
scrub. Many months after some men found a
something sitting on a log, with its feet in the
creek. Higher up, boots were picked up. Then
shirt, pants, waistcoat and hat at intervals.
Curious, isn’t it? —they all strip. A surveyor’s
field-book was found in the coat-pocket.
In the earlier days, a Canadian named Madden
left the Pieman for Mount Bischoff. He was
supposed to have had 80 ounces of gold with him.
He, too, is one of the lost. Afterwards, on a tree
not far from the main track, this legend was
discovered: —“Going North. In good spirits.—
Madden.”
A creeping kind of story hails from Deloraine.
A Mrs. Gleeson wandered forth to look for a cow.
She has not yet returned. Two dogs accompanied
her. One trotted back next day. The other was
absent for a considerable period, and returned
hearty and fat. Perhaps the most satisfactory
course to pursue, in the interests of the bereaved
relatives, would have been to bury the dog.
A friend of mine, pottering about the Western
Tiers, found a skeleton in a small sandstone cave.
It was negligently leaning against the back-wall,
and was a well-preserved structure over six feet
long. The police supposed it to be the remains of
one “Dido,” wanted by the authorities over 25
years ago. Starvation or insanity baulked the
cell of its victim. Numbers more have been
brought in, and they will continue to arrive for
many a long year to come.
But what a wonderful sight it would be were
the Great Invisible Power pleased to inaugurate
a Grand Skeleton Revival. Fancy the old fossils
rising wearily up from behind rotten logs;
from beneath the decayed vegetation; from
the crannies of the rocks and the beds of
the creeks; from the depths of luxuriant
fern-banks and the scarcely trodden mounds
of the button - grass plains ! Fancy them
clattering down the hill-sides, down the rocky
gullies, tottering along the muddy pack-tracks
down to the main roads of life. Some bearing
their lower jaws in their fleshless fists. Others
with bundles of ribs beneath their arms. Here a
feetless image doddering along on stumps. There
a headless monster feeling its way among the tree-
trunks with shaking fingers. Toothless, eyeless,
grinning ghouls, without a rag to cover their
nakedness, creeping to the haunts of men, with a
silent request to be decently planted. A sight to
shock the world—and a rich harvest for the
coroners.
Tas.
C. H. READ.
The “Thirsty Land."
( FOR THE BULLETIN .)
Out from the city when work was slack,
By poverty crushed and by ill-luck banned,
With an empty pocket and burdened back,
He “ tackled the track ” to the Thirsty Land.
Out to the West he went for work,
And never a heart was his to shirk ;
With a strong right arm and a willing hand,
He shouldered his “drum ” to the Thirsty Land.
Back in the city ’mid smoke and din,
With spirit broken and hope grown weak,
A woman sat: and the hand was thin
That brushed the tear from her faded cheek.
With never a friend in that mighty crowd
To whisper of silver behind the cloud,
She plied her thread with a quivering hand,
And prayed for the one in the Thirsty Land.
Over the plains where the shimmering haze
Floated before him along the track,
Scorched by the sweltering noontide blaze,
With blistering feet he tramped “ out back.”
Weary his steps, yet he must not lag,
Scant are the drops in his water-bag;
But he lifted the bag with a trembling hand,
And he drank his last in the Thirsty Land !
Brave was the heart that had struggled on,
And it never “caved ” when the end was near;
But he sank in his tracks when his strength was
gone,
And the crow’s wild call fell on his ear.
Fell on his ear as his blistered eyes
Sightlessly turned to the pitiless skies ;
And his fingers clutched at the burning sand,
As he yielded his life in the Thirsty Land,
Back in the city of smoke and din.
Crushed by a longing that ever is dumb,
The woman waits when the post comes in,
Waits for a letter that never will come.
Feb. 8, ’94.
T.H.W.

Dear Bulletin, —Yes, The Bulletin, of
all journals issued in Australasia, is the one most
frequently remailed from one reader to another.
It is also more frequently stolen in transit than
any other paper. My news-agent’s account for
1893 requests me to pay for eight copies which
have been reordered to replace missing numbers.
I get a few other papers which come regularly
enough. It is The Bulletin alone for which I
have to suffer. — Waihoa, Maoriland.
The unceremonious and unfeeling style in
which only too many Australian capitalists, in-
cluding the N.S.W. Pastoralists’ Union, are
cutting down their employes’ wages on the “ take
it or leave it ” principle, recalls nothing so mueh
as the way in which the Army Hospital orderly
soothed the dying moments of the poor soldier
left to his tender mercies. “’Opkins,” sez I,
“ you’re mortal bad.” ‘‘l am,” sez ’e. “’Op-
kins,” sez I, “ I don’t think you’ll get better.”
“No,” sez ’e. “’Opkins,” sez I, “ you’re going
fast.” “Yes,” sez ’e. “’Opkins,” sez I, “I
don’t think you can hope to go to ’eaven.” “ I
don’t think I can,” sez ’e. “Well, then, ’Opkins,”
sez I, “you’ll go to ’ell.” “I hope so,” sez *e.
“ ’Opkins,” sez I, “you ought to be werry grate-
ful as there’s a place perwided for you, and that
you’ve got somewhere to go. Then ’e died.”
A Maoriland teetotal potentate, just re-
turned to Christchurch after a long journey, gives
his experiences :
I arrived at my first “Temperance Restaurant ” at 7.30
p.m., after a long, wet journey. I was received by a
voluminous female, wearing a blue ribbon and a heavy
scowl. “Can I have some tea?” I asked. No, I could
not have tea at that time of night. “ Can my clothes be
dried?” No, they could not; the fire was out. So I
crawled up to bed and hung my damp garments on the
back of the only chair in the place. I thought I would
have a drink of water before turning in, but there was
no water-bottle in the room, and the fluid in my ewer
looked pea-soupy. At the second “ Temperance Hotel,'*
which I struck on the following evening, the feeding was
dirty, the hash full of the landlady’s red hair, the air of
the proprietors was haughty and austere, the partitions
of the rooms were thin, the rooms were small, and the
bed and blankets were stained, but there were several
fly-blown texts on the wall, and I was invited to attend
*• family prayers.” After these experiences I sojourned
at seven different hotels where drink was sold. I received
every comfort and attention, was not, as at the last
temperance hotel, kept awake by noisy boys, and, speak-
ing generally, though I detest drink as much as ever, I
trust decent hotels will not be abolished until the pro-
hibitionists have shown that they have some constructive
skill by establishing temperance hotels which are not
outrages upon comfort, cleanliness and decency.’’

correctly supply the word missing from the

response to this advertisement.
Only one guess is allowed on each Coupon,
but competitors may send as many Coupons
as they please.
Coupons received to Saturday, 28th
April, 1894. J
The missing word is placed in a sealed
envelope in the custody of the manager of
the Bank of New Zealand, Sydney, and the
contents are known only to the manager of
this paper.
If the word is not named, the money will
be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded
by post-office order or stamps of any pro-
vince ; when the latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.
